This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-

and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
fte has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may bo eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to hi j
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes. Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general
weakness, and other symptoms which lead to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for he has

SATURDAY, APRIL 11, 1891.
Constitutional Treason.
The draft Australian Constitution now being
considered in Convention has at least one
merit —almost unique as a feature of any
document formulated by lawyers only—it is
terse, perspicuous, and a model of simplicity
of expression. Those pernicious involutions
of phrase with which it is one of the tricks
of the lawyer's trade to enwrap alike both
fact and fiction, are distinguishable by their
absence from almost every chapter and sub-
section. In whatever form the Constitution
may be adopted, and whatever may be its
result in legislation or upon the interests of
the people, the men who framed its scaffold-
ing will have demonstrated that it is possible
to clothe legal enactments in terms capable of
“being understanded of the common people. ”
Chief-Justice Higinbotham, in pursuing his
work of consolidating the Victorian Statutes,
kept constantly in view simplification of
phrase and exactitude of definition. He
brushed aside the fetich of tautology before
which the medicine-men and the swindling
augurs of the Law have been accus-
tomed to compel the people to bow down,
and we are glad to perceive that the influence
which the great democrat—great lawyer, but
greater man—still exercises over the thought
of his generation, exiled though he is to the
judicial bench, has extended to the Conven-
tion. The very non-ambiguity and precision
of wording makes, however, the dangerous
effect of the draft Constitution more palpable
and patent. Had the document been as
complex, as involved, and as absurd in point
of phrasing as the majority of the Acts
which, a heritage from the dark ages of
Britain, burden the Australias with an im-
ponderable mass of obscurity, there would
have been no inconsiderable chance of some
fatal clause passing unnoticed at the present
day and leaving a legacy of doubt and
disaster to a later time. We wish we could
as highly praise the Bill as a statesmanlike
attempt to grapple with the novel conditions
under which an enduring Federation of the
Australian “ Colonies ” alone can be effected.
As a coherent expression of an advanced
national policy, the draft falls immeasurably
short of its opportunity, and presents just
those interstices in the materials of its
structure which enable the representatives
of the Classes to import the tools with which
they mean the destruction of the democracy.
The Class-men have lost no time in utilising
the gaps.
* * #
Necessarily, before these words appear in
print, the draft Constitution will be altered
in several particulars. But it may safely be
prophesied fcjqp provisions of the
draft* ;mTai n'S’i'ill e d tl l eywnUi >c' amendecT'in
a direction the reverse of that where stands
the Australian ideal. The resolutions pro-
posed by Sir Henry Parkes did not face,
and the draft does not move a foot, so to
speak, towards the Federated Republic
which is the ultimate goal of the Australias.
The propulsive force was, however, expected
to be manifested in Convention when the
Constitutional Committee reported. But
the amendments effected up to date have
driven the scheme farther away from the
goal of true and intelligent Australian poli-
tical thought. The trend of the Conven-
tion’s stops is Imperialistic, not Australian :
the men themselves arc betraying both their
own past and tire Australian future. There
is scarcely a man among the Convention—
we speak by the book and can, if need be,
give chapter and verse—but has, at some
time or other, declared publicly his convic-
tion that the ultimate destiny of these
countries is separation from the Mother-land.
He has done so from no extraordinary
power of prescience, but simply because the
most inert politician has been acted upon by
the subtle acid of the time, which is biting
away at one and the same moment both the
Throne and “the bonds which link” the
dependencies and the Throne. If these men
are sincere in their belief that the evolu-
tionary forces at work in our political society
must sooner or later disintegrate the “ Em-
pire,” then where is their claim to
statesmanship, where, indeed, their claim
to be regarded as commonly honest, if they
do not with such wisdom and such power as
political experience and knowledge of life
confer upon them, smooth the path to the
goal of Australian aspirations ? If they are
honest men they are untrue to their past
and to the representative position which
they now hold and which they presumably
gained because of their past. And if they
claim to be guided by the comprehensive-
ness of view and perception of principles
which form the statesman, then how can
they explain their relation to the irresist-
ible power which, overruling the mere
mechanism of politics and politicians, is
quietly and naturally forcing upon the people
of the Australias a policy of national inde-
pendence ? They can only prove their
honesty at the expense of their intelligence.
Independence will come, is coming, in spite
of the Convention and the politicians. Hie
politicians can block the way, if they choose.
They may in doing so cause disaster for a
time. But they will no more succeed in
preventing access to the terminus than the
wretch who chocks a log on the railway-
lines stops the nation’s rail traffic.
# # *
That the majority of the Convention are
doing their best to upset the Australian
coach as it travels on the route of Nationalism,
in the childish hope that it will then be
turned round and start anew for Imperialism,
is now apparent. The divisions on the
motions to make the Governor-General an
elective official, and on the proposal to base
the federal franchise on the principle that
one man shall have one vote and no more,
are incontestable signs of this shameful
desire. An elective Governor-General is a
preliminary to the final freedom of these
provinces, or, pursuing the figure we have
already used, a mile-stone on the road to the
Australian goal. At the present moment
this mile-stone, this stage, could, if the
Convention so willed it, be reached by the
people of the Australias without perceptible
effort, without any rude straining of the
“ Imperial bonds,” and without any particu-
lar violation to the Imperialistic instincts.
The elective Governor-General would not
necessarily be an Australian ; he might, in-
deed, be a peer of England ; even one of
those exemplary and intelligent personages,
the Princes of the Blood Royal might
be chosen. Surely an election of a
man from the “ Empire ” at large,
rather than from the Federated States
only, would have a much more potent influ-
ence on the relations of the “ Empire ” and
the “Colonies ” than his mere nomination—
which might be the price of a party treachery
or of a woman’s honour—by the British
Ministry “or the man Rogers ?” To such a
suffrage a Gladstone, not to say a Dufferin
or a Dilke, might appeal without lowering
his personal prestige. All the world over,
an honour conferred by the popular will is a
thousand times more precious than one
awarded for reasons which cannot, in
decency, be divulged. Viewed from the
Imperialistic standpoint, an elective Gover-
nor-General might, it is conceivable, be the
more desirable institution. Viewed even from
the Australian standpoint, there is no ques-
tion as to his being an essential institution.
What, then, we have again to ask the mem-
bers of the Convention, or, rather, that huge
majority which out-voted Sir George Grey,
Dr. Cockburn, and Mr. Kingston, have
they to say for the decision which, so
far from bringing the Australias a stage
nearer the peaceable consummation of
every true Australian’s hopes, goes the other
way 1 The special, correspondent of the
Melbourne Argus, who is known to be hand
and glove with the leading Conventionists,
cannot be suspected of a partiality to demo-
cratic views, yet he declares that the number
of Convention members who thought with
Sir George Grey, but voted against him,
was larger “than many would believe.”
The Bulletin, from its personal knowledge
of many of the delegates, affirms the same
thing. When some Bancroft arises, fifty
years hence, to tell the story of the founding
of our Constitution, what will he say about
'those palsied guides and purblind leaders of
the people who resolutely turned their backs
on the path indicated by the finger-posts of
patriotism, statesmanship, and honour, and
wended their way in the contrary direction 0
By their devotion to Imperial interests,
by their lack of courage to act up to their
convictions, the men in Convention have
unquestionably made it plain that they
entered into a series of compromises. The
negotiations have not been published but,
nevertheless, they have taken place—the
parties to immoral traffic seldom proclaim their
transactions. There are compromises, and
there are compromises. There is a c mpro-
miso of mere expediency which is (he balance
of details and particulars, and there is the
compromise of principle, which is.simply an
attempt, foredoomed to failure, to balance
right and wrong, or good and evil. There
can be no compromise in morals ; the bases
of ethics arc unshifting, and stand ‘‘four-
square” and immoveable to every “ wind of
doctrine.” From the law that for every
deviation from the straight line there shall
be incurred a penalty, nations and societies
are not exempt any more than individuals ;
neither States nor individuals can palter
with . ethical truth without suffering. It is
plain, we say, that there have been compro-
mises upon compromises among the Con-
vention members, and we would be the first
to applaud them were the points involved
merely those.connected with administrative
procedure, and not problems co-relaled to
the functions of morals. Upon such a point,
for instance, as the selection of a site for a
Federal Capital, compromise may be not
only desirable but necessary, and therefore
wise ; sq, too, with regard to the proportion
of senatorial representation or of tariff-
adjustment. But when it comes to a ques-
tion of freedom, to one concerning the right
of self-government, to one of the mainten-
ance of simple honesty in public affairs, the
matter is altogether different. Upon a
question of the kind compromise is impos*
sible, for wrong can never be right, any
more than white can be black. You cannot
have half-tones in morals.
# # #
Has, however, the question as to whether
the Governor-Generalship shall be an elec-
tive post or not, a moral aspect ? Is it not
an abuse of terms to describe it as
originating in or having a relation to
morals 1 These are interrogatories which
the Imperialists have put, and they them-
selves shall provide the answer. The
Governor-Generalship must be either a
principle or detail —something that is
either indispensable to the Federal struc-
ture, or something for which something else
may be substituted Avithout perceptible
loss. Now, the Imperialists declare—we
quote from the Argus, than which no
journal is more effusively “loyal' 1 to
the Imperial connection--that to refuse
the Crown the power to nominate
the Governor-General, Avould mean “the
hauling-doAvn of the national flag,” and
that his election by the Australian people
would virtually “ cut the painter.” A pro-
ceeding which might bring about these
dreadful contingencies cannot belong to the
category of mere details ; it is vital ; it is
essential; in short, it is a principle. Treat it
as of a secondary importance only, and you
Avould, on the Imperialists’ oavii showing, err
grievously. Force the Imperialists into the
corner, and they agree with Sir George
Grey and the Radicals Avho are proud to
follow in his footsteps, that the method by
Avhich the Governor-General is to be ap-
pointed is peculiarly a question of principle.
Between questions of principle and questions
of pure ethics, there is never any dividing
line. They dovetail imperceptibly.
* # *
This question of the Governor-Generalship
derives its importance, and the conduct of
the men who voted against making the post
elective, its treasonable character, from the
consideration that a nominated Governor-
General must be a sham and a falsity.
Parkes, moved by that poetaster's senti-
mentalism of his which sometimes mocks
him by leading him to the very verge of a
luminous truth, but oftener drives him to
the depths of mawkish inanity, clenched his
main argument at the Melbourne Conference
for a “Federation under the Crown” by
remarking that “the Governor-General of
Australia would be able to hold a Court that
Avould be as attractive as that of the monarch
of the old Avorld, which is not a light thing
to be passed over as a matter of sentiment.”
Right at the root of the Nominee Viceroy-
ship lies this simulacrum of a “ Court ” rvith
all its parasitisms and all its falsehoods. A
man in Avhose hands the people place the
charter of its executive power’ — a Lincoln,
a Garfield, a Grant —Avould, almost cer-
tainly, be a man who Avould estimate
“society” at its true worthlessness. Between
his rugged, perhaps toil-hardened palms,
“society” would be chary of placing its
Avhite gloves, afraid that the immaculateness
of the kid might be stained by a drop of
honest sweat; but a Viceroy Avho had “kissed
hands ” on receiving Her Majesty’s commis-
sion, Avho, perhaps, had been permitted to
dig the Heir-Apparent in the ribs, to back
his Royal Highness’ bills, and to cash his
Royal Highness’ 1.0.U.’s at the baccarat-
tables to a Governor-General of this
class a “ Court ” would be necessary, and
courtiers many. It is to our shame that
they would be many. The vice-regal
system Avhich has obtained in Australia to
the present day has been socially degrading.
It could not have helped being anything
else. It has produced a breed of syco-
phants ; it has set up false standards of
living; it has made, or has sought to
make, a card of entree to Government
House a certificate of manly and womanly
virtue. But the vice-regal system which the
Constitution will create, inasmuch as it will
be more distinctly aristocratic, will intensify
the resultant evils and the resultant shame.
“So long,” said Sir George Grey in Con-
vention, “as there are Governors-General
sent here from England, so long, I believe,
there must be inevitably an aristocratic
British Party resident in Australia.” The
venerable Pro-Consul declared the true Aus-
tralian mind in these words. A nominee
Governor-General will be surrounded with
an aristocracy which will look to Britain for
sanction of its social developments, and will
esteem political distinction only as it is
branded with a titular “ honor.” It will be,
however, a class that will bo deficient in the
one feature which can ever be admirable in
an aristocracy, for that high-breeding of the
individual which is the product of the
leisure and culture of generations will be
wanting here. Our “ Colonial ” aristocracy
will not be the less but the more pernicious
because it does not bear the hall-mark of
“ long descent ” and inherited instincts. It
will be a privileged caste without the one
quality which can redeem the privilege.
# # *
Now, in the Australian Commonwealth
there must be no room for caste or a privi-
leged class. The p ditics of the twentieth
century will be sociological ; it will be the
happiness of society in the mass and in the
unit, and not the aggrandisement of dynastic
or plutocratic sections, that will be the con-
cern of the State. The hour will soon strike
when the cry of an infant at its starving
mother’s milkless breast will stir the heart
of the nat'on quicker than a thousand Im-
perial rescripts. In the sphere of the new
politics the born aristocrat shall have his
share of justice, and his opportunity ; the
brat born in the gutter shall have no less.
This is plainly written on the walls of the
present social structure, and the men who
shut their eyes to the inscription invite the
fate of Belshazzar. Deep sunk into the in-
telligence of the masses everywhere is the
truth of Bakounine’s remark “ that it is
the characteristic of privilege to kill the
mind and the heart of men.” The privileged
man, whether politically or ecoiromically, is
a man depraved in mind and heart. This is
a social law which admits of no exception,
and is applicable to entire nations as to
classes, corporations and individuals. It is
the law of equality, the supreme condition
of liberty and humanity. And because
a vice-regality will sustain an existing
privileged class and create new ones
the Australians must have none of it.
“ The Governor-General will only be a
ceremony,” said Parkes in Melbourne ;
“ the position will only be a bauble, a gilded
toy,” said Deakin in Sydney. How can the
viceroy be a mere ceremony or an idle
bauble, if he hold the power to veto the
will of the united Australias ? But suppose
Parkes and Deakin are correct, we ask—
What do the Australias want with mere
ceremonies, empty baubles, or childish toys?
Are our men children, or are they the
founders of a nation which is to precede the
world to the tablelands of regenerated
humanity? If the former, then why stop
at one Sham ? Why not bring along a
gaudier ceremony and an emptier bauble
still ? Give us a peculiar share in the
Crown. Make us a kingdom, and “Victoria,
by the grace of Goi>, Queen of Great Britain
and Ireland, and Empress of India,” also
“ Queen of Australia !” The Conventionists
have only to act on this hint to assure them-
selves the peerages which are at present in
the clouds,
* #
The treason to the Australian future,
which the Convention effected by its decision
on the proposal to make the Governor-
General elective, does not stand alone. It is
emphasised by the vote on Dr. Cock burn’s
attempt to make the franchise of the Federal
Parliament essentially democratic. So con-
temptible a shuffling with the plain issue is
rarely witnessed in any deliberative assembly.
Creating a Federal Parliament, the Conven-
tion are calling to existence a distinct body of
electors, whose franchise must, therefore, be
defined at the outset. This duty is no
function of the State. If it be, then nine-
tenths of the talk in Convention as to State-
right was useless and mischievous. The
Convention, by a majority vote of 28 to 9,
referred the Federal franchise to the several
States —in other words, declared that there
should be a Federal Union without uni-
formity or identity of constituency. This
vote in itself was not treason, may not have
been even insincere, may simply have been
a misjudgment of the position. But when it
was effected under the influence of motives
which the majority had not intelligence
enough to disguise, and which was nothing
less than the desire to prevent the operation
of absolute unqualified manhood suffrage
throughout the Federation, it can only be
properly designated as treason to the Com-
monwealth. In every State at the present
time, with the exception of South Australia
and New Zealand, the property-vote is
the master of the political situation,
even in the popular chambers. Its influ-
ence in Victoria is partially modified
by the circumstances that the elections are
held on the one day, but elsewhere it
retains its destructive supremacy almost un-
challenged. Impose upon the States a
function which is not theirs, which has
nothing to do with their State independence,
and the proceeding is tantamount to giving
the multiplied property-vote preponderance
with the Federal Parliament. This the


Convention has done knowingly, with a
clear apprehension of the significance of its
act; it has denied Australian manhood, as
manhood, the prime prerogative of citizen-
ship—the right to a voice in the election ot
its rulers. There is but one term to describe
this deed : Treason.
* * *
These provinces are democratic communi-
ties, and for their representatives to juggle
the’democracy out of the opportunity to
exert its full strength on any question vital
to its interests is as traitorous an act as
could be performed. Parkes, amidst the
applause of the majority, declared that
the Convention was not the proper place
to enunciate the true democratic doc-
trine. “At the proper time he would
be prepared to deal with the question.”
Does any sane man believe Parkes, trickster
and opportunist, will ever arrive at the
proper time till the people force him ? How
did he regard the “ one-man-one-vote ” idea
in New South Wales till Mr. Traill was
backed by the popular judgment? “All
they have to do,” he went on, “ was to bring
the Federal Parliament into existence, it
could find the means of giving shape to its
own electoral system,” At the base of the
Federal Parliament must be the elector.
Who will trust the Federal Parliament,
fettered by the States and the Constitution
and elected by the faggot vote, to declare
the right of* manhood to electoral power,
when the Federal Convention, unfettered
unexcept by self-imposed bonds, repudiates
so vital a principle ? “It was almost pre-
sumptuous,” he concluded, “for the Con-
vention to declare what that electoral system
should be.” If the Convention may not lay
the foundation, its effort to build the super-
structure is an unjustifiable aggression. But
no one, not even the men who applauded
him, believes Parkes meant these things.
All know he was only posing ; Jennings,
with his woolly intelligence, and Fitzgerald
with the soul which is an emanation from his
own vats, know it; the propertied people, of
whom Jennings and Fitzgerald are the
mouth-pieces, know it. They know, too,
that when Parkes was followed by 27 Con-
ventionists into the lobby, the vote was safe
for the time being, and that once again
democracy has been betrayed by its pseudo-
friends.
* * *
The duty of the Australian electors is now
plain. They must insist (1) upon the Con-
stitution, as finally passed by the Conven-
tion, being submitted to them for ratification,
amendment, or rejection ; and (2) that a
second Convention shall be held to consider
State amendments. Sir George Grey has
tabled in Convention a motion for the sub-
mission of the Constitution to a plebiscite of
the people. This motion was no sooner pro-
posed than, as The Bulletin has been
informed, heads were counted, and it was
concluded that it would not be adopted by
the Convention. It would be a surprising
fact if it were, considering how the Conven-
tion has already damned itself and its work.
But not merely the plebiscite must be in-
sisted upon by the people, but a referendum
in detail to the electors. Such a course will
be denounced, of a certainty, as “Un-
English ” ; it will be the wiser and the truer
for that very reason. It is because the Con-
vention has been so English that it has
betrayed Australia.
Universal Ignorance.
A cvilised nation of modern times is described
by Ruskin as a motley gathering of humanity,
which resolves itself practically into three
elements. First of all there is a mass of discon-
tented, penniless, and half-taught, or less than
half-taught, inhabitants, which calls itself the
people; secondly, a thing which calls itself a
Government, meaning an apparatus for col-
lecting and spending money; and thirdly, a
small number of capitalists, many of them
rogues and most of them stupid persons, who
have no idea of any object other than money-
making, gambling, and animal enjoyment. This
description was intended to apply exclusively
to the so-called civilisation which has grown up
in recent centuries, but practically it has been
the history of all nations and all ages. Of the
three classes, at least two have always been
present—the Government in its capacity as a
machine for collecting and spending money and
an instrument for executing anyone who
objected to its existence ; and the people, the
dull, ignorant mass of human bodies and alleged
human souls that submitted with dreary, ox-
like patience to innumerable sufferings and
exactions, because unable in its ignorance
to comprehend the possibility of a state in which
these elements were wanting. As for the
capitalist, he is a variable quantity and
dependant upon circumstances. The first
business of every country is to support a
Government and to have enough left over
to keep the population alive and in working
condition in order that they may continue to
support the Government, and if there be only
sufficient to fill these requirements the capitalist
class is non-existent; if there be anything more
the capitalist is always present i. > take the
balance. Should the balance increase, the
capitalist and the Government scramble for the
surplus ; should it diminish, the fight between
these two parties assumes a more frenzied
aspect than usual because they are both
ravenous and both insatiable ; but the difference,
so far as the people are concerned, is very
slight—in the first case their situation is about
as bad as it can be, and in the second it is a
little worse The one privilege possessed by
this third party in the State is that it never
becomes extinct—a privilege which it shares
with the horse and the cow and other indis-
pensable animals; for the Government have
something to govern, and the Government and
the capitalist must both have something to live
upon, so they combine to keep the nation alive
for purposes of revenue. And the one class
which endures everything and pays for every-
thing, accepts the situation with tolerable con-
tentment, because in its fathomless ignorance it
is unable to realise the possibility of anything
very much better.
* # *
The world has lived on its ignorance for many
centuries. It has done what it was told to do,
and has thought what it was told to think with
persistent regularity, and it is about as ignorant
in the present age of enlightenment as it was in
the days before it was enlightened. It has
made immense advances in education within
recent years, and its verb agrees with its nomi-
native more than it used to do ; it knows more
ancient kings and ancient battles, and has
acquired a better idea as to the exact situation
of many inaccessible mountains with unpro-
nounceable names, and bottomless swamps with
names that are long and incomprehensible, and
ungetatable rivers in regions which cannot be
reached, and yet its ignorance goes on with
very little deviation. It has acquired sundry
dead languages and much equally dead arith-
metic, but all this knowledge has had very
little effect upon the condition of the great
mass which is commonly dominated the People.
Even the spread of democratic institutions has
only altered the outward semblance of things,
and the bulk of humanity still goes out enthu-
siastically to be shot in quarrels from which it
gains no benefit, and gives way to bursts of
frenzy over political changes which lighten none
of its burdens, and pays the bill as joyously as
of old when it hears that it is necessary to whale
some alien race—in fact, it does what it is told
to do almost as slavishly as it did in the days
of absolutism. Even among the most intelli-
gent of nations nine-tenths of the population
are without any intelligence, except such as has
been gained at second-hand; they take their
inspiration from the politician with the loudest
voice and the longest hair, and it lasts till some
still louder individual supplies a fresh set of
opinions in its stead.
* * *
The progress of the Irish question in England
is a conspicuous example. Ten years ago
Liberals and Conservatives were practically
unanimous on the subject of coercion. Glad-
stone gave his vote for coercion in those days,
and his party voted as he told it to vote, and
all Great Britain did the same. In all England
and Scotland there was hardly a solitary con-
stituency which did not regard Parnell and
his party as a gathering of depraved insurrec-
tionists, and there were but few English or
Scotch members in the Commons who did not
take the same view. Then came Gladstone’s
great recantation, and straightway half Eng-
land went bald-headed for Home Rule, and at
least one-third of the House of Commons fol-
lowed suit. They had all howled for Union
because they had been commanded to howl for
Union, and, in like manner, they howled for
Home Rule when their political bosses in-
structed them to do so ; and yet had the fiat
gone forth from the Conservative instead of the
Liberal camp, the Tory party would have
whooped for Parnell with almost equal fluency.
And, in like manner, when Dalley conceived
the idea of sending a contingent to Africa, he
did not appeal to any existing opinion or offer
to supply any existing public want. The
country had never felt that it was necessary for
its health and comfort to go forth and kill an
Arab, and spend a vast sum of money in the
process—if there had been any such want it
would probably have been aware of the fact;
but the glib politician merely issued the state-
ment that New South Wales was pining to
spend the money and to kill the Arab, and the
great mass of the local population, having no
opinions of its own and no knowledge except
such as it received at second hand, at once
accepted the announcement and acted accord-
ingly. A million human beings were assured
by one fluent orator that they were boiling over
with loyalty, and though they had no previous
conception of the fact, they straightway boiled
over out of the depths of their own fathomless
ignorance—in fact, they acted as they were
told to act, and the slaves of an Asiatic despot
could have done no more; indeed, it is ques-
tionable if they would have done so much.
In an intelligent and democratic community
somewhere about one-tenth of the population
possibly attempts to think on its own account
without much success ; in a community which
is neither intelligent nor democratic one man
generally does the thinking for the entire nation,
and does it badly, and if he finds any un-
authorised individual taking a hand in the work
he executes him as being a person of dangerous
and revolutionary tendencies. The whole
course of politics in all countries is based on the
assumption that humanity always exercises its
alleged intelligence by proxy. When Parkes
stumps New South Wales on the Freetrade
ticket he does it on the plain, unvarnished
theory that the colony has no brains or opinions
or resolutions of its own, and that for want of
these attributes it will vote at his instructions ;
and when Dibbs stumps the same country on
the Protectionist ticket he acts on precisely the
same axiom. If the populace vote for Parkes
in preference to his opponent it merely demon-
strates that Parkes’ voice or his eloquence or
his size or his hair or some other attribute has
created the greater impression, but it certainly
does not demonstrate that the nation has any
fixed views in favour of Freetrade, for if they
possessed any such views then the necessity for
Parkes to go on the stump would vanish. The
openly expressed conviction that Freetrade will
end with Parkes is an open acknowledgment
that when Parkes is gone the party will not be
able to raise another orator with the same
capacity for beguiling the country into voting
against its Protectionist convictions—if it has
any —or else it is the expression of a fear that
the Protectionist party will then have the
advantage in the matter of fluency and gush,
and will thus induce the populace to vote
against its Freetrade opinions—supposing it to
possess any such substance. In like manner it
is freely asserted in England that the cause of
Home Rule depends on the survival of Glad-
stone for a few years longer, which is a candid
admission that the English elector has an un-
biased opinion laid on in his house in the same
manner as gas, and when Gladstone dies his
successor may lay on an altogether different
opinion, and thereby change the entire course of
British politics. That the elector should have
any definite opinion of his own, except on the
most elementary subjects, never enters into the
calculation, and the visionary, who starts on
the assumption that the world knows anything
except what it is told to know, is certain,
sooner or later, to be “ left.” And even Glad-
stone, the Tribune of the English people, is
travelling on a second-hand conviction lent him
by Parnell, and if Parnell had not existed
there would probably have been no Home Rule
party in England at all.
* # #
Public opinion is in the main the force of
aggregate ignorance, guided partly by heredity,
partly by concussion, and partly by infection.
The European is nominally a Christian because
he was told to call himself one by another
European of the previous generation and the
name has become a matter of habit; and Pro-
testantism, Catholicism, and other branches of
the faith have all their separate strongholds
merely because the Protestant has no fixed
reason for being a Protest, and the Catholic is
a Catholic without any visible cause, except
that their respective ancestors so directed them.
The rampant Abigail howls for Orangeism
owing to the passing chance which caused him
to be born in a certain place and under certain
circumstances, but there is no inherent force in
Abigail which would have made him what he
is apart from these circumstances; under
another set of conditions he would have been
as intolerant in the cause of Confucius as he is
now in the cause of Dutch William. Here and
there, at long distances and at longer intervals,
the world produces by chance an original
thinker who saves the human race from becom-
ing stereotyped and utterly stagnant, but their
number is lamentably few. Mankind, in
general, consists of a governing class which
governs according to certain old traditions
because it is incapable, in its ignorance, of in-
venting new ones, and a subordinate multitude
which submits by force of habit, because, despite
its increasing education, it is too ignorant to
know how to resist, too dull to possess any
coherent opinions except those which are sup-
plied to it in a ready-made condition, and too
stagnant to know anything beyond a craving
for some reform which it cannot define. It
creates a revolution, now and then, and is un-
able to decide what to do with it when it is
made, and it overturns a despot here and there,
and from sheer force of habit permits another
despot to grow up in his place, and it is plun-
dered and exploited by rings and syndicates,
capitalists, monarchs, politicians, and priests
because it has been plundered through all the
ages, and the descendants of the horse-leech
have become so completely a part of its existence
that it lacks even the knowledge and the
originality to dispense with them.
The Australian community is probably one of
the most enlightened and progressive at present
existing upon earth, and a liberal system of
education has given to the inhabitants of this
continent many advantages which are unknown
in most other lauds. Consequently, the Aus-
tralian citizen has aquired a large and general
knowledge of football and acquatics, and his
information on the subject of horses and bruising
is eminently reliable. As for his political
economy, it is mostly acquired at second-hand
from Parkes and other equally uncertain
sources, and his religious convictions are partly
derived from the imported bishop or priest,
who has little to dispose of, and partly from
the imported Orange lodge, which has none at
all. He has certain vague misgivings about
Socialism, because, in the first place, he has no
distinct idea what it is, and because, in the
second, he has been informed by Bur-
dekin or the Argus, or some similar
authority, that it is a nefarious project and
eminently unworkable. Also, he has sundry
aspirations regarding the abolition of land-
monopoly, if he only knew how to begin, and
if he is a draper he is loyal on account of his
miserable salary, his shiny boots and his collar,
whereas if he is an artisan he is Republican for
various other reasons. He is a self-governing
individual, and he governs the country pretty
much as he is told, and those from whom he
takes his instructions know less of the subject
than he does himself, and yet even they are
vastly superior in knowledge and intelligence
to the great majority of humanity. The strong
point of this world is its wide and general
ignorance, and what it does know it mostly
acquired by word of mouth from some exceed-
ingly uncertain authority, and he usually
diffuses the information because it was handed
down to him from his ancestors, and how they
gained it, or whether they ever gained it at all,
no one can tell.
“Freedom of Contract.”
B. R. Wise, with his thrust at the “fetish of
freedom of contract,” has thrown a firebrand
among thorns, and the air is filled with the
crackling thereof. With alternate vacuous
guffaw and windy vituperation the capitalistic
press has thrown itself afresh into the battle for
the inalienable right of the employer to make
what terms he pleases with his men ; and all
the resources of journalistic objurgation have
been strained in the effort to cope adequately
with the iniquity of the Queensland shearers’
supposed attack on the sacred principle of
fr-'c lom of contract. As a matter of fact there
is no such attack. Freedom of contract is no
more at stake in the strike of the Queensland
shearers than is the law of gravitation or the
dogma of papal infallibility. Moreover, the
question could not be at stake there, because no
such thing as freedom of contract is at this
moment open to the Queensland shearers. The
attitude of the hysterical section of the daily
press on this subject may be the outcome of its
habitual mendacity, or it may be merely
chuckleheadedness ; probably it lias some con-
nection with both.
* * #
Obviously there can be no freedom of con-
act except when the contracting parties
are equally free—equally dependent on one
another, or mutually independent. In Queens-
land the organised importation of black-
leg labour has destroyed that essential condi-
tion. It has placed in the hands of the squatter
a weapon to coerce the shearers into compliance
with his terms, and has made real freedom of
contract an impossibility. Lot there be no mis-
take about that. Trade Unionism, which has
brought labour on to something like equal con-
tracting terms with capitalist monopoly, bids
fair to find its match and more in the systematic
organisation by capital of the power of the
blackleg. If it is to regain its ground it must
look for some means of eliminating the black-
leg, and this it can only do by destroying the
system of which he is a product.
# * *
The blackleg, in essence, is the superfluous
man, the man on the outer margin with the
black sea of starvation on one side, and a star-
vation wage on the other. Since first the wage-
system was coupled with monopoly—one class
seizing the patrimony of the race, and imposing
on the other class a heritage of paid service—
the blackleg and his prototypes have been the
toiler’s scourge, “The poor always ye have
with you,” has bitter truth for the man who has
earned his bread for a master in the sweat of
his brow. Wherever and whenever the man
on the margin has been willing to take, or has
been forced to take, a starvation wage, he has
set in motion the blind, merciless engine of Un-
restricted Competition which inevitably crushed
every wage-earner down to his own level. That
was the evil that trade-unionism, with its
weapon the strike, was designed to resist. For
a time it succeeded. It clogged the wheels of
competition, and though the man on the margin
was still there, he ceased to be effective as an
instrument of oppression in the hands of mono-
poly. Freedom of contract in some measure
became possible. True, it was still in the
power of the employer, as before, to say:
“Take work on my terms or starve;” but the
labourer, entrenched behind his union, had now
the reply: “ Employ me or your land will not
be tilled, your sheep will go unshorn, your
loom will be idle, and you must face ruin.”
Ruin on one side, starvation on the other,
brought the parties to a fair footing.
* * #
But the balance has been again disturbed.
A conspiracy of monopolists has brought the
blackleg once more to the front, made
him once more a factor to be reckoned
a menace to his class. The man who
will take, or must take, a starvation wage
has found his old place, and recommenced his
historic function, except that instead of his
being pitted, as of yore, man to man in the
cruel race of competition, it is now array
against army. Competition is the weapon of
monopoly against labour ; Unionism, in so far
as it was a check on competition, was labour’s
sole defence ; and if monopoly can once more set
competition free, it must inevitably get labour
again under its heel. When that happens,
freedom of contract will make a long farewell,
only to come back, if ever, on the bloodstained
wings of revolution. That is what Unionism
in Queensland is struggling against. That is
the future foreshadowed to Queensland labour
by the army of blacklegs, organised, drilled,
handled by capitalist monopoly. Nor is it the
battle of Queensland alone. Let Unionism be
crushed there, and the same tactics will have
force to crush it in New South Wales, and in
every part of the continent,
# * *
It would not be unnatural if the Queensland
shearer, turning out to meet this new invasion,
with the prospect before him of being thrust
again into the abyss of cut-throat competition
out of which Unionism had done so much to
lift him—it would not be unnatural if his feel-
ings should take the shape of personal resent-
ment against the personified image of the
threatened oppression—the blackleg who had
taken the enemy’s shilling and enlisted in the
campaign of coercion. It would not be wonder-
ful if he were tempted to take up the challenge
which the sycophantic organs of capitalism
whoop out so exultantly on the departure of
every new batch of his supplanters. But the
battle now is not to be won by the direct con-
flict to which his enemies are so anxious to
tempt him. Tire odds are too great. The
employers have learnt the lesson of combina-
tion and organisation, and if the struggle is to
be a peaceful one, the capitalist, with the power
of concentrating a marshalled mob of blacklegs
on any given point, must win. It is the story
of the Franco-Prussian war over again. The
side that could always count on bringing 10,000
men to the spot where an engagement was
to take place with 5000 of the enemy, was
pretty safe in telegraphing to Berlin the news
of its victories the day before they came off.
* # #
If the struggle, wc repeat, is to be a peaceful
one. It is too early yet in Australia to seriously
contemplate anything else. Tire appeal to lire
and sword lies always ready to the hand of the
oppressed as a last desperate resort, but no one
thinks of picturing Australian unionism or any
section of it, as reduced to the pitch of despair
—not even, we hope, those who have conceived
the deplorable project of shipping 5000 shearers
to the Argentine Republic. Let the leaders of
federated labour put their foot down there. It
cannot be borne that one ounce of the bone and
sinew of the country should be driven out in
confessed defeat in the struggle with monopoly.
That, in the long run, would only intensify and
prolong the class war, and postpone the ulti-
mate victory, the victory that lies in store for
the world’s workers when they leave the shifts
with which in the past they have gained some
temporary amelioration of their lot, and boldly
attack the forces of caste and monopoly in their
stronghold. We want our men in Australia,
and if anyone has to go, we can spare the
Argentine republic half-a-dozen shiploads of
bloated capitalists better than half-a-dozen
pairs of the sinewy hands whose toil has helped
to lay the foundation of the future Australian
nation.
»- -■» - t . ji
The time to come may have in store a day
when the workers of Australia find themselves
really at bay, between the devil and the deep
sea, driven to look across the ocean for a place


of refuge. That day is not yet, and we can
barely conceive that it ever will be ; but if such
a day should dawn on this continent there
would be justification enough, backed by scores
of hoary and sacred precedents for a revolution
whose blaze would make the Reign of Terror
pale its ineffectual fires. We are not likely,
however, to find ourselves in need of the
guillotine here. The Australian voter is armed
—and those who are still voteless by reason of
capitalistic laws will soon bo armed—with a
weapon more deadly to monopoly and privilege
than the firestick or the “ bushnian’s sister.”
Just now his business is to get hold of the
ballot-box and learn what he never yet has
learned, how to use it. How to combine and
ivork out one thing at a time.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Australian Fleet.
Australia’s half-built iron-clad tieet is going
the way of iron-clad fleets in general. The
latest news concerning the Karrakatta is that
she has gone out on three successive trial trips,
and has broken down three times, and so far as
evidence goes the other broken-winded dredges
are in about the same condition. It is the same
old yarn that we have heard so often before.
The noble craft starts from her moorings like a
thing of life, and after trampling down two
yachts and a lighter with her big coarse feet
the steering gear at last begins to work, and
she proceeds on her way over the waters, while
the oysters run ahead to keep out of the dust.
Then the demon of incarnate recklessness in
the stoke-hole piles on the coals, and shoves oil
and grease into the furnaces, till at last the
procession starts to annihilate time and space
at the rate of two yards in six weeks, and a
minute later she blow's up and fires six men into
the blue vault of heaven, and their families
have to be compensated. It takes three months
to do the needful repairs, and when the un-
happy vessel gets to sea once more she breaks
down and floats about helplessly for 40 days,
while grass grows on the deck and a blue mould
accumulates on the admiral; and then a
schooner laden with condemned fish takes her
in tow and drags her into the nearest port.
Then she is patched up some more, and blows
the feet off most of her crew while at gun-
practice, owing to the fact that the guns fire
backwards and explode at the wrong end, and
the rest of the ship’s company die of old age on
the voyage. Still she does get to her destina-
tion at last, if she has anything like good for-
tune, and some day the Australian people will
see a pile of old rust and scrap-iron and howling
incapacity on the far horizon, and will know
that their expensive fleet has arrived. And
after that the trouble will begin in real earnest,
for the Australians have only had their country
to defend up till now, whereas in future they
will have to defend their navy as well, and if
an enemy comes along they will have to
fight like demons to keep the crippled
old thing from being captured. As a rule,
when a man of any intelligence goes out
to fight another man larger tnan himself, he
doesn’t take all hi» paralysed female relations
along with him to hamper his movements, and
when Beach or M'Lean goes to row for a cham-
pionship he seldom puts his consumptive grand-
father in the boat for the sake of his assistance
and encouragement; yet this is exactly what
the Australians are doing. They are going
to pile up several million pounds’ worth of
helpless and incapable property in an exposed
situation, and then sit up and watch it, and so
far as the defensive aspect of the affair is con-
cerned it would probably pay about as well if
the money were put on a raft and anchored out-
side the Heads. A fleet which breaks down so
unanimously in calm water, when it is quite
new, may be a handy thing to put old admirals
in, but as a means of heaving defiance at the
world in general it doesn’t amount to much. It
has been killed at the start by too much British
government inspection. The one unfailing
peculiarity about every vessel built under close
official supervision is that it can’t go half a mile
without sinking, whereas any private company
can get a ship built that will sail the seas for
forty years and hardly meet with one serious
accident all the while.

The Manufacture of History.
“ The present disturbances,” writes an aggres-
sive Queensland, paper on the subject of the
shearers’strike, “ will be in after years one of
the blackest spots in this colony’s history.”
The Bulletin firmly believes that they will,
and not only will they be one of the blackest,
but in all probability they will be the very
blackest and ugliest spot of the whole lot. The
two things that are wanted in the manufacture
of history are one man to write it and another
man to believe it, and the sort of history that
is written is exactly the sort that people are
willing to pay for. The history of the shearers’
“insurrection” will not be written by one of
the strikers, for shearers, as a rule, are not
historians ; nor will it be written for the
strikers, for the average shearer does not buy
histories. But some day it will be printed —if
the class now on top is to have its way—in a
volume marked “for the use of schools,” and
some howling Tory squatter, with his soul full
of bile and aggravation, will supervise the work
in his capac.ty as Minister of Education, and
will see that the gall and the curses and the
adjectives are all correct; and when he has
relieved his feelings with all the language at
his command, his alleged information will be
soaked into the minds of the rising generation,
and will become history. The world is full of
that kind of history, and it is all marked by one
reliable and never-changing feature—that the
party of the man that wrote it was the
bravest and noblest and most beautiful and un-
speakably moral party on earth, and that every
other party was a gang of hideous and diseased
ruffians of the meanest type. The ancient
Greeks started upon this system, and so long as
they wrote all the records that were going,
their invariable peculiarity was that one Greek
—and not a very large Greek either—was able
to scatter an army single-handed, and that the
Greek nation in general knew everything, and
did everything, and conquered every place, and
defeated and killed everybody on the whole
earth. The world has accepted this race for
2000 years at its own valuation ; and yet the
whole Greek people doubled up like a web rag-
in the pages of history the instant another
nation began to do the writing, and their
courage and glory and patriotism were all sold
for old junk on the instant. Their successors,
the Romans, lasted until other nations began to
write about them, and then the dignity and
grandeur, which had weathered so many storms
so long as the empire fixed up its own history,
all went to pot. The Turkish State is coming
to grief to-day for want of more vigorous and
vociferous writing. It is stronger, more
tolerant, more civilised, more progressive, and
a lot cleaner than several of the Christian
kingdoms of Europe, but the Turk is not a
literary character, and his history has been
written for him by hard-lying individuals who
want his territory, until he has become an out-
cast, and it is the duty of all the world to jump
on him. History, in its own erratic and
unaccountable way, made a monster of Lucrezia
Borgia, and though all the evidence goes
to show that she was one of the most
spotless women of her age, it rose superior
to evidence. It made a brilliant genius
out of Elizabeth of England, whose kingdom
was defended by public subscription, because
that meanest and most stupid of crowned
harridans would not even pay the wages of her
own sailors when an enemy was off her coasts,
and it has advertised the same miserable female
as the apostle of religious freedom, regardless of
the conspicuous fact that she was, with one
exception, the most brutal persecutor who ever
reigned in England. And considering these
and a thousand similar episodes, The Bulletin
will be surprised if the shearers’ 44 insurrection”
is not one of the blackest spots in the annals of
Queensland, for it has a good general knowledge
as to the sort of individual who will write these
annals, and history depends, not on the circum-
stances, but the man who dishes-up the circum-
stances and lies about them. And when that
story comes to be written, if it be the interest of
a large and influential governing class—which is
able to hire and pay for the necessary literary
talent — to prove that Parkes was not a man
but a horse, then Parkes will be a horse
accordingly, for the thing that history can’t
prove is not yet created.

The bull-headed J. B. Patterson, of Victoria,
curses his old worn-out curse at trades-
unionism: 44 1 say that because a baker may
have some dispute in Loudon, therefore a
butcher in Edinburgh should join him to carry
out what he is engaged in—l say that is the
invention of the devil.” And on the other
hand, Patterson, supposing a monarch
should have some dispute in London, and
should haul the country into war over some
racket of her own, and a butcher in Edinburgh
should refuse to pay his share of the bill and
say that he would see the potentate taking in
washing before he would waste his substance in
propping up her German relations—how would
that be, O ignorant and cantankerous driveller
with the iron jaw and the fishy eye? And sup-
posing Patterson himself were assaulted in tfle
street and was being jumped on with iron-shod
boots, and nobody would interfere because, by
his own showing, interference in another
man’s disputes is an invention of the devil
—how would that answer? For if it is immoral
and deviLsh for one man to help another to
defend himself against an over-reaching capi-
talist, it is equally immoral to help him to de-
fend himself against a plain street rowdy, and
when The Bulletin sees somebody putting
both his feet in Patterson’s eye it will re-
member that gentleman’s words, and leave him
to attend strictly to his own interests. A prin-
ciple is no good unless it is logically carried out.
Even the system by which an employer in Bris-
bane joins a union and undertaxes to help an
employer in Sydney or Adelaide to 14 carry out
what lie is engaged in ” looks somewhat like an
41 invention ot the devil ” when you examine it
closely ; and when a Melbourne shipowner has
a dispute on, and a butcher or a tailor comes
swarming down, say from Echuca, to bear a
hand in his capacity as a volunteer or some
other gaudy idiot with a gun, that seems also
to be an act of the same infernal character.
And if Patterson’s own grammar isn’t an in-
vention of the devil it is only because that per-
son was tired and in a hurry, and left the job
toffiis apprentice.
On Monday, 6th April, the Federal Convention
reached the crucial clause of the Constitution
Bill, and definitely settled it in a way by which,
we venture to say, the majority have earned
the execration of future generations of Aus-
tralians. By 22 votes to 16, the Convention
decided to refuse co-ordination of power to the
Senate. If this is to be final—and so far as the
Convention is concerned it is necessarily final—
the bloody problem of secession will loom on
the Federal horizon as soon as the requisite
number of States enact the Constitution. To
effect unification is to develope a monstrous
centralisation, which would be tantamount to
handing over the executive power to a syndicate
or ring, such as has controlled the government
of Canada for the last twelve years. And not
only that. Whether it is a good thing or a bad
thing, the fact remains that the several colonies
have developed, each within its own sphere,
certain peculiarities of social construction, com-
mercial progress and industrial development.
No Federal Constitution can annihilate such
characteristics ; what the Convention had to do
was to study and adjust, them—so far from
doing this the denial of State-rights means
the absorption of smaller States by the larger.
Without co-ordination of power the individual
States will have their legislative individuality
nullified by the prevailing tendencies in the
representative chamber. It is said by the
opponents of State equality in the press and in
Convention that the denial of State-right is
essential to the preservation of the absolute and
ultimate will of the democracy. The Bulle-
tin, on the other hand, distinctly avows its
belief in the co-ordination of the States because
it asserts that the democratic principle would
be in danger by subordinating the State-cham*
ber to the House of Representatives. It can
best define its position, and what it thinks
the illogicality of the opposite view, by a simple
arithmetical formula. A New South Wales
elector, a unit of the largest State in the Com-
monwealth, will have 3ff-130ths of representa-
tion in the popular house; in the Senate he
will have l-7th. A W.A. elector, a unit of the
smallest State in the Commonwealth, will have
4-130ths of popular representation, but he has
equally with the other, l-7th of Senatorial. So
far as regards the popular Australian interests,
the man of the lower State is relatively as
amply protected as the unit of the largest, but
in the case of his peculiar and individual
interests, those which are originated by his
residence in his own State—such as, to take an
illustration from America, the interests of a
New Orleans citizen which are now attacked
both by the “ Mafia ” and the Federal Govern-
ment—he has a superior representation to
which he is entitled by reason of the fact that
his peculiar interests and his individual liberties
must ever be dearer to him than the interests
of any other man, even a brother-Australian.
The State equality is the appliance which pro-
tects each individual elector in his dearest and
most intimate concerns. The Conventionists
have been regarding the States Senate not as
representative of distinct political entities
which must remain distinct, but as the tra-
ditional Upper House of the English system.
They could hardly help blundering on this
rock, simply because they were taking charts
laid down by British navigators for their home
seas, with which to pilot the Australias through
the unknown waters that environ the Aus-
tralian future.
+
The Grand Lodge of the Loyal Orange Institu-
tion of New South Wales has given the
“ glorious memory ” of King Billy a rest and
has declared in favour of an Australian Re-
public. In other words, it has solemnly recorded
its disapproval of any form of Australian
federation that “would tend to imperil the
existing relations between these colonies and
the British Crown.” This will be a cruel blow
to the scheming English politicians and the
bogus Australian ditto who have been bribed
with British baubles, and who have been
trying to boom Federation with the one
object of bursting up those same “ exist-
ing relations.” They did count on the
holy Orange mob to howl the loyal- chorus
about pulling tighter the silken bonds, and 10,
the Grand Lodge says: “No, thank you; we
don’t want them pulled tighter. We don’t
want to imperil the existing relations.” That
is just what The Bulletin says. If we are
going to federate, let ns leave our relations
with Great Britain just as they are. At present
the gate of Independence is standing open just
at our elbow, and all we have to do is to step
out when we like, and bid our mother-in-law,
England, an affable “Good morning.” We
don’t want any federation that will imperil that
state of things. But we know, and the men
sitting up in the Macquarie-streeet convention
know, that the vision of ribbons and stars and
baronetcies dancing before their eyes is not
going to materialise itself as a prize for leaving
things as they are. There is a nice little
conspiracy against Australian interests at work,
and we rejoice to see that the Loyal Orangemen
are on the track. They are not going to stand
by and see this young nation trapped into a
lopsided partnership that it might not be able
to scramble out of for generations.
An Ohio (U.S.) politician has drafted a bill
providing that any criminal condemned to
death in that State shall be allowed to choose
one of four modes of extinction, by being hung,
shot, poisoned, or drowned. This is an idea
that some enlightened M.L. A. or M.H.R. might
take up with advantage. If the State insists
on retaining the barbarous policy of capital
punishment it has, at all events, no particular
interest in deciding how the offender is to be got
rid of —it wants to get rid of him anyhow, and
that is all. It can’t borrow any money on the
security of the melancholy “ thud ” which it is
so fond of, and so long as the prisoner is ex-
tinguished it ought to be satisfied. There is a
dreary sameness about the old, mouldered
gallows, and the noseless executioner who
blandly asks his client to 4 4 stand over a bit,
please,” and the greasy rope, and the rest of
the paraphernalia ; and many a blase homicide,
who has used up all the different sensations and
dissipations of life before he took to assassina-
tion, would be glad to try something fresh. If
he prefers to go out into the yard on a bright,
cool morning, and let the State’s man-butcher
plug him with a gun there can be no earthly
reason why his last desire should not be
gratified ; and if he wants instead to be loaded
down with lead, and dropped into a cold,
refreshing tank, the outraged law would get
there just the same. In the matter of poisons
he might be subjected to certain restrictions;
for instance, if he wanted to perish through
some corrosive substance which would make
him yell for 48 hours before he departed, his
request might reasonably enough be refused, for
no man has a right to make himself a common
nuisance ; but anything that would cut him off
without causing unearthly howls should be per-
missible. There would be a little pleasing
variety about death under such conditions as
these ; it would serve to rouse the hangman out
of his moral stagnation, and make the hang-
man’s assistant more spry and limber; and it
would cheer up the sheriff, and brighten the
pensive countenance of the gaol flogger; and
the grave-digger would feel that a faint ripple
had passed over the putrid sea of his dead intel-
ligence. Possibly it might even help to gladden
the chaplain—who can tell ?

At Sydney Her Majesty’s “My Jack”
has turned up trumps after all. Folks who love
to weep even if their feet get wet thereby, who
delight to have their souls harrowed by fiendish
threats, theatrical cyclones or earthquakes and
narrow escapes from blood-curdling horrors,
crowd the auditorium nightly. The circle, how-
ever, doesn’t exactly froth-over into the stalls;
vice is only popular in that part of the house when
practised by a duke and savoury of the divorce-
court, and virtue, especial y when unfortunate
and tearful, has for it few charms. Should we
ever write a melodrama for the dress-circle, it will
contain a land-boom, a gubernatorial lev6e, lots
about “the silken bonds,” several titled
“Johnnies” and heaps of champagne. Arthur
Greenaway, who strained himself through a stage-
accident on opening night, has been obliged to
retire, and his place is tilled by Guyton Heath,
who, if not so Claude Melnottish, weeps with
equal facility—tears that are somewhat more
oleaginous. “The Corsican Brothers ” are getting
very fit in the hands of George Kignold, Edith
Bland et cie and Harry Jewett has got Chateau
Renaud “ down very tine.”

According to popular report, “Bridget
O’Brien, Esq.,” is “ the best place to have a laugh
in town.” That being the general impression,
biz. is good at Sydney Garrick, where Vincent
West’s expansive smile suggests the ticket-office
and his spiked moustache is a stumbling-block to
deadheads. A strong feature of the show is the
bright, lively music. That of the preliminary
movement to the Sappho ballet, it may be remem-
bered, was transcribed from the phonograph by
Conductor Robertson in Melbourne. Perhaps the
best vocal item is the “Little Peach ” duet, be-
tween Johnny Sheridan and Gracie Whiteford,
first made known to fame, if we are rightly
informed, per medium of Ered. Leslie and Nellie
Earren at London Gaiety. Now we want the
Widow to tell us why she lets the play fall with a
sickening thud while the variety business takes
the Hour? This hiatus breaks the continuity and
interferes with the riohoray iffict av the tooton-
shamble. The Bovis’s “ Dora, Dorer, I love you,”
is a clever item, but their costoorn suggests a
bier-garten, and their slippers are a moving
menace to Day and Martin. Signor Imano is in
such good voice that it is a pity he ham t more
singing to do, and the Widdy’s only fault is that
she is not enough in evidence. No change in the
bJI is mooted.
** m *
The Sydney public have been so often
deceived by illusive preliminary puffs, that we
were inclined to view with incredulity the flatter-
ing press-notices from South Africa and England
that arrived here in advance of the Misses Albu.
Having heard them, however, we are conquered,
and freely admit their excellence. It is rarely
that you find so much technical skill allied to such
vocal freshness and power. Julie, the taller of
the two, is a willowy young woman, graceful and
of fine stage presence, who has a clear, resonant,
highly-cultivated voice of great range and flexi-
bility. Her chief fault is a straining for effect,
chiefly shown in sustained dwelling on finals.
One specimen of long drawn-out sweetness as
a sample oi reserve force is admissible,
but with frequent repetition effect is lost and
monotony achieved. This is especially true of
ballad-singing. Simplicity is the ballad’s key-
note, sympathy its predominant feature. Rose
Albu’s voice is lower-set than that of her sister;
it is fuller at its base and slightly metallic at its
apex and probably will eventually develope into a
true mezzo. But it is also highly cultivated and
managed delicately and with much feeling. At
their opening concert these young ladies were
assisted by Miss Apollino Niay, a scholarship
holder and exhibitioner of the Royal Colleg®
of Music, who has lately settled down is
Sydney to teach the pianoforte. Miss Niay has
evidently a thorough acquaintance with the techni-
calities of her profession, and should be well
patronised. The Y.M.G.A. concerts, although
the Albus were supported by fair representatives
of local talent, were not successful from any but
an artistic point of view, and they open this even-
ing (Thursday) at Sydney Town Hall where it is
to be hoped that they will reap the reward that
exceptional natural ability, when allied to the
artistic skill born of severe discipline, deserves.
The accompanist is Miss Naylor.

“Dr. Bill” has established a wonderful
practice at Adelaide, and his pat ents are
thoroughly satisfied with his mode of treatment,
even although the company that handles Aide’s
farcical comedy is in almost every respect inferior
to the B. and B. interpreters. Teddy Royce is
funny enough, but streets away from George
Anson. Inman, as Dr. Bill, doesn’t seem to
thoroughly grasp situations, and Harry Diver,
who appears as George Webster, looks silly
enough for admission to any lunatic asylum.
Charlie Brown, as Mr. Horton, is appropriately
boisterous, while Edwin Kelly as the servant
helps to keep the fun up. Marie Brooke has
made a great success as Miss Eauntleroy, but
Mrs. Walter Hill as Mrs. Firman, is usually “ not
at home.” Polly Emery is a capital Ellen, but
Maud Appleton’s monotonous voice goes sadly
against her. Myra Kemble found it necessary to
absent herself for one evening, and her part was
undertaken at very short notice by Miss Harrie
Ireland, “ Our Fiat ”is underlined for early

At Bondi Aquarium last Saturday, a
boisterous day, Fernandez went up about 150 ft.
in a rent balloon, and then slowly dropped to
earth in the glen just above the lawn, leaving the
nasty, smoky balloon and attendant parachute to
drop behind a house on the tennis-lawn, much to
the disgust of the players.

Each of the Melbourne theatres was able
to renew its bill on Saturday night, a most un-
usual state of things in these hard times. But
ere the present issue of The Bulletin has ap-
peared, in all its rosy splendour, above the horizon
there will be another change at the Bijou, where
“Masks and Fac s” was to discontinue a mild
career after Tuesday. Of the people employed in
dragging the heavy stage-coach along the old ruts,
only Miss Emma Bronton grew fresher as they
neared their journey’s end. Most of the others
began—in the language of sporting literature—to
hang out signals of distress, which were received
by select audiences in a spirit of reciprocity.
Even Miss Achurcli’s effort on the light fantastic
too (at Triplet’s lodgings) seemed to get more
fantastic, and less light, with each successive
struggle, and the advertising of this sombre
sh. file as an Irish jig reads like another nasty
jar for Parnell. Enough of such sad memories,
h wever. Ibsen’s “D ll’s House,” that beautiful
story of a mother’s love in three rollicking acts,
was due last (Wednesday) night, and perchance it
may run for more than the allotted three perform-
ances. Many Melbourne women are seeking a
sound philosophical-reason for giving the old man
an 1 family the slip, and if they like to hurry up
and sit at the feet of Ibsen, bedad ! they will hear
of something to their advantage. Possibly, too,
there is a general desire to gaze once again upon
the entertaining person who wants to slink away
into a dark corner to die of his hereditary disease,
and the associated bank-clerks of Melbourne
certainly ought to rally round their Norwegian
brother in affliction, who talks so cheerfully of
descending to the gutter when he receives a week’s
notice. Fortune may smile, as the scoffers are
sure to do, upon this revival of “A Doll’s House,”
and in that case there will be no necessity to pro-
duce “The Wager” on Saturday, for the last
week of the Charringtonian season.

Still harping on the Bijou— the matinee
benefit to Miss Aston was the huge financial
success anticipated by all careful students of
sassiety sympathy. In the artistic way Snazelle,

mm m m
“Moths” is getting its due measure of
appreciation at the Royal, for only the very upper
regions were other than crowded out on Saturday
night. The shilling pit has been resuscitated with
good results, and with this return to the old order
of things has come an all round state of pros-
perity, the more remarkable because it is so well
deserved. There is an air of thoroughness about
this handsome show. The ladies are elegantly
accoutred for the giddy fray, and Sass shoots his
linen with the same calm deliberation that charac-
terises Cautley’s handling of his hat. Also, the
last-named artist has a way of inflating his chest
which stamps him as a songster out of the
common, and although the audience have to take
ms phenomenal warble for granted, they feel that
the heaving bosom carries conviction with it. An
opera-tenor whose notes combined the sweetness
of the nightingale with the rich swell of an organ
(vide Ouida) would no doubt have a demonstrative
bust like Cautley’s. Prince Zouroff’s pants are
less satisfactory than Correze’s palpitations, but
then it isn’t the tailor who makes a royal Russian
libertine, and Charles Cartwright, considered
apart from his wardrobe, is a genuine success.
Miss JNlethersole, too, has somewhat toned down
the mannerisms that mar her beautiful perform-
ance ; whilst all the other roses retain their early
bloom and fragrance. “Moths” is indeed a
fascinating entertainment, containing as much
polished naughtiness as one can reasonably expect.

‘ I alka ’ appears to be crowding Mel-
bourne Opera House night after night, notwith-
standing that the opera gets but a moderate
reception considering the many pairs of hands on
the premises. The more subtle signs and indica-
tions of a prolonged run are not there in fact, but
as the management constantly announces “ House
r nil m bold characters, without exceeding the
strict truth, there is every reason to suppose
that they feel happy and thank their “stars”
accordingly. The show sails down a rippling
stream of melody on an even keel, and if the wind
of public favour showed a disposition to abate,
the steam-tug Age would be towing it along all
the same. John Gourlay is less highly chalked
and otherwise a more taking Tancred than on the
first night. Why Falka (being a romantic school-
girl with a soaring imagination) should elope with
an odd-looking youth rather shorter than her
little self remains a mystery dark as ever, but the
chorus of sixty voices is delightfully comprehen-
sible, ditto a largely-augmented orchestra and
other first-class fittings.

by taking a great farewell benefit on Friday night.

after a twelve-months’ tour in search of novelties,

Strange incident at London Royal Italian Opera
recently. Madame Roze, in the boxes, missed a valuable
diamond brooch. A man saw it drop right into the
bi east-pocket of a stalls magnate, who was quite aghast
■when he drew it out, on being appealed to. This beats
conjuring*.
Contractor David Mitchell (the diva's papa) scratched
his head a good deal when he read the cable-lie that the
Due d’Orleans had been foo.ing around disguised as
Madame Melba’s valet. The old man might well look
pensive. In the first place, who (save a chuckle-headed
sub-editor) ever heard of a woman having a valet, as a
valet in the English sense of the word ?
Comic opera has a strong hold on Melbourne. When
“Tom Jones” and the “Gondoliers” were young together
they seemed to form a mutual improvement society in
business, and now “ Pepita ” and “ Falka ” oppose. The
rival stage-managers are Bracy and Wallace. Bracy has
the latest London experience, but then Wallace 'dates
from the Noachian deluge, and so it is a toss-up espe-
cially in affability. Which do we like best—Wallace's
st.ile or Bracy s now which? It is choosing between
Lord Chesterfield and Sir Hubert Stanley.
“Agitate ! Agitate !” Such was the hoarse whisper of
property-master Wyatt, of Melbourne Theatre Royal on
his death-bed in Alfred Hospital. It is the familiar’ex-
clamation of the prompter, calling on his myrmidon to
waggle the proscenium door, lash the pit and gallery into
fury, and secure a call for the star.

dividend, it is illogioally argued that two more tne
Crucifixion and siege of Paris must succeed also at the
same time.
