An Open Letter.
(To the Proprietors and Editors of the New
South Wales Press.)
Bentlemen, —You, if we are to believe your
own words and the eloquence of post-prandial
orators, occupy a position in our state second in
point of potency to none. I, who venture to
address you, am simply what my signature
imports me to be—a creature who waits on
your beck and call, to whom your smile is bread
and butter, and your frown starvation. A cat
may look at a king without timidity, but a
scribbler may not approach you without a
quickened heart-beat and a tremulousness of
the knees, for the scribbler is less than the
grimalkin, whose living may be obtained with-
out sacrifice of independence. But you are
greater than the King. For you are, gentle-
men, are you not, the Power behind the
throne ?
# * *
You are, gentlemen, peculiarly the Annalists
of the people. Noble function ! —to record with
a single eye to truth and to posterity (which
can never rightly know itself unless it under-
stands the time out of which it has arisen) the
deeds of the present. You are, also, the
Teachers of the People. Nobler function still !
—to guide the unthinking masses, to stimulate
the dormant intelligence, to suggest the good,
to correct the evil. And once more, gentlemen,
you are the Defenders of the People’s liberty.
Noblest of all functions ! —nobler, indeed, than
that of the Fidei Defensor, for a faith is a
limitation, a restriction on liberty ; and the
liberty to know, to utter and to argue freely,
according to conscience, is the highest preroga-
tive of the human identity. You assert this
and are prepared to defend it, are you not, so
long as you have an ounce of printing paper left
for a wad and a leaden type to act as a bullet'?
These functions are yours, gentlemen, and never
yet were greater or more momentous associated
with any mundane institution. Pope or Kaiser
never claimed .efficacy of a diviner sort. And
never since the Dark 'Ages has there been Pope
or Kaiser who had his claim admitted as fully
as the popular voice has sanctioned your claim.
The people have learnt the lesson your fore-
runners suffered to teach them —that in paying
a penny for a newspaper they purchased not a
broad-sheet, but a, Palladium, an MCgis, and a
Gonfalon.
* * »
This power and these functions are yours,
gentlemen. There is not one among you but
has at some time exercised the power and
assumed the functions, and neither is there one
of you who has refrained from proclaiming to
the uttermost bounds of your area of publicity
the fact that you have done the one and the
other. The meanest rag ever issued by the
most contemptible of your members —a man
may be a journalist, a teacher, and a defender of
public liberty, and still be personally con-
temptible (Marat was all these things)—has
boasted again and again that it was at once the
repository of popular rights, the shield of public
liberties, and a standard flaunting in the van of
progress. The boast is, of course, well founded,
but even if it were not, a statement only
requires constant reiteration to be installed as a
clause in the creed both of the men who make
it and the people who hear it.
# * *
Filling these onerous and benignant functions
and administering this tremendous power, no
one has ever yet taunted you with abusing your
right. You have shown yourselves almost
superhuman in the massiveness of your self-
restraint. The attitude of magnanimity, whose
lack flaws the most lustrous fame, can justly be
claimed by you. Owning a giant’s strength,
you have scorned to use it like a giant. For
when did you ever lift your united hands to
wrest from the State a privilege, or from the
Law an immunity ? When did you ever abuse
the public confidence ! When did you
ever exert your own forces to secure
a concession for your class ! Among
the whole series of classes which Mother
Earth has spawned you stand alone. You are
unique. Never, since the first historian penned
his first lie has there existed a class which so
persistently declined to attract to itself and its
parasites such good things as were going; never
' one that, holding a monopoly of power within
its grasp, let it fall as though it were valueless;
never a class which, constituting also a craft or
profession, neglected to entrench its business
interests behind the bulwarks of etiquette;
never a class which, entitled to take an inch,
did not consciously strive to seize an ell. To
you, gentlemen, as a class, which is also a pro-
fession, it has been reserved to manifest this
abstinence. No one would ever dare to cast
upon you the imputation that your class inter-
ests and monopolies endanger those of the rest
of the public. You are magnificent in your
magnanimity.
* * *
But you will, perhaps, pardon the query ; Is
it not possible to have too much of a good
thing? May magnanimity itself not prove a
weakness if carried to an extreme ? In an age
which is nothing if not mercantile and competi-
tive, is it altogether a virtue to refuse oppor-
tunities of strengthening a professional position
when, with professional interests, is bound up
the wider welfare of the public at large •
Remember, gentlemen, you do not stand or fab
alone. With you in decadence or progress must
go the people, for are you not their Annalist,
their Teacher and their Defender ? To weaken
you as a class and a profession is to weaken the
State, and in your admirable anxiety that the
State should not suffer from any undue aggres-
siveness or selfishness on your part, have you
not gone too far towards the other extreme ?
Have you not fallen short of that middle point
which the commonest instinct of self-respect
will define as the meeting-place of just assertion
and unjust aggression ? If you have, then the
State is already injured, for your obligation to
it demands that you shall maintain yourselves
in unsapped strength. You can sin by weak-
ness as well as by presuming on your might.
Of the two, in this world of hard knocks and
strenuous wrestlings, the sin of weakness is the
more shameful.
# * *
And, gentlemen, this more shameful sin is
yours. Your magnanimity is magnificent, but it
is that of a lick-spittle. You have been so
tenderly solicitous of popular interests that you
have forgotten your own good and your own
dignity, and in so forgetting have overlooked
the fact that when the interests of the humblest
individual in the State (even of a hireling
scribe or the proprietor of a newsrag) are un-
protected, the interests of the State as a whole
are endangered just as the fruit is unsound
when the tiniest spot of corruption specks the
rind. Moreover, how can you be strong enough
to guard popular rights and popular liberties
when you are so weak that you invite the
popular contempt for yourselves as a class and
that a profession? Now a re-action has set in and
that you are the object of public ridicule and dis-
dain, instead of being regarded with public
reverence, is patent to every critical mind. This
is the reward of your abstinence and your
magnanimity, and I for one am not going to say
that it is not richly deserved.
* # *
For if you were strong in self-respect and
conscious of your own dignity you would never
“ADVERTISEMENT FOR A NEW INFALLIBLE BALSAM.”
GOVERNOR HOPETOUN.
BEFORE USING.
AFTER USING.
for one thing have permitted the iniquitous Press
Laws of New South Wales to remain on the
Statute-books. For another you would never
have accepted that half-condescending toler-
ance which public men extend to you. For a
third you would never consent to live in the
pockets of advertising-agents, and at the mercy
of the “ mammoth ” advertisers who for a full
“ double-column ad.” can now purchase your
bodies, and, with a “half-page till counter-
manded,” could buy your soul.
# * *
Let us come to the point. The other day a
man—a hired scribbler —-“doing time” for an
offence against a Press Law, and under a sen-
tence so brutal that it startled even the prose-
cutor, was removed from Sydney to Maitland
gaol ironed by hand and leg to a felon. In
prison clothing, fettered and manacled, and in
the glare of the public gaze, this man was trans-
ferred from one prison to another. There may
have been “valid departmental reasons” for
this outrage; there are “valid departmental
reasons ” extant for the prostitution of imported
females which are only not in force because there
are no transported females to apply them to.
But whether there were reasons or no reasons
is not the point. The point is that you—you,
the members of a profession engaged in the
noblest of tasks, of a class entrusted with the
most momentous of public functions—have
allowed this outrage to pass unnoticed, not to
say undenounced. May I ask what insult, if
any, would arouse you to a sense of manliness ?
& # #
Reseigh Martin is now to be released after
serving one-half his monstrous sentence. No
thanks to you; you almost unprotestingly
allowed him to go to gaol for an offence which
every one has committed or is liable to commit
by the purest accident at any moment—he
gave credence and publication to a float-
ing rumour which thousands of people
beside himself believed—and as a class you
have never moved the length of a barley-corn
to secure his release or to relieve the sufferings
inflicted upon his innocent relatives in conse-
quence of a sentence which not even the most
callous or brainless of you declares to be just.
That accusation awaits your answer, though
Reseigh Martin should be freed while it is
being framed. Your answer. But dare you
answer it ? When . the Authorities treated
Martin as a felon, they registered an imputa-
tion against your class—your profession. V\ hen
they shackled him they simply put into use, on
the dormant spirit of the Press, laws which date
from the time of the First Blackguard of Europe,
and which were framed to meet the exigency
that arose when bold men dared to tell ‘ ‘ that
sceptred Thing,” that profligate and liar though
he might be in private life, as a monarch he
ought not to be a scoundrel and a perjuiei.
And when the Authorities ironed Martin, they
constructively placed the “ darbies ” on you, oh
brother journalists ! If Martin wore them
justly, then not one of you but should be simi-
larly decked.
* * *
The Authorities literally put fetters on the
journalist. Figuratively they do the same
to you every hour of every day, and you accept
this position. You, the “ Fourth Estate, the
Leaders and Teachers of the people, the
Defenders of Popular Rights and so forth have
not sufficient esprit de corps, professional in-
stinct, or sense of self-respect to assert your-
selves and your liberties under the most brutal,
unjust, and shameful of insults.
A Hireling Scribe.
It is said that several “ teams ” of bottled
beer have been started along the overland tele-
graph line, S.A., from the railway-terminus, to
meet the Kintore expedition from Port Darwin.
The teamsters reckon they will unload all their
trade when they “ How-1-found-Livingstone
the vice-regal caravan.
Some W.A. Chinese lately quarrelled
over a game of cards. One of them feeling
agrieved sharpened up the tomahawk and went
for ” the other three heathens. The first man was
out into mince-raeat, the second was deprived of
two ears, a leg, and an arm, but the tomahawk-
man seeing the third Celestial making tracks for
the bush transferred his attentions to the owner
of the flying pigtail, and when he was done with
him the one thing wanted was the undertaker
Result: two deaths and one mutilated survivor
who may live to give some explanation of the
tragic event. The strangest part of the affair is
the fact that the murderer promptly walked into
the nearest township and gave himself up to the
police.
Business in almost every line may be
deadly dull, but the “ guinea-pig ” vocation
flourishes like a green bay-tree. A certain
N.S.W. silver mining property, in the early days
of local silver-raining somehow got itself into a
financial tangle, and to rectify matters it was
decided to let the mine on tribute. This decision
was carried into effect, but the salaried directors
stick to their posts as of yore. They kindly act
as receivers of tribute, and pocket fat fees for
doing so. They sit regularly once a fortnight, and
humbly divide a trifle of about £lO between them.
The business transacted at these meetings suffices
to kill about five minutes valuable time, which
means a cost to the Co. of £2 a minute or so. The
names of the directors deserve to be recorded in
letters of brass.
Sophia Gunsberg, the last prominent
Nihilist who came to grief, was evidently an
inventive female, and if she had kept her head in
the crisis of her conspiracy there would have been
an opening for a new Czar. She was a beautiful
woman, with innumerable lovers, and she con-
ceived the brilliant idea of sending them all out —
unknown to each other —to prospect for the Em-
peror with guns or bombs or poison, having first
assured each one that she would marry him when
Alexander was wiped out. In the course of a few
weeks she had about a dozen young Russians on
the Czar’s track, and among them all his chance
would not have amounted to much—only it hap-
pened that at the supreme moment Sophia went
the way of all womankind, and really fell in love.
Instead of sending her last mash along with the
rest to go shooting at a monarch, she eloped with
him ; and when the other dozen heard of it they
grew dissatisfied and gave the scheme away.
They gave Sophia away, too, and did it so badly
that they saved nothing out of the wreck—not
even themselves. The end of it was that the lady
is imprisoned for life in a dark, damp cell, and
the twelve or fourteen youths who used to buy
hot chestnuts for her, and hang round her house at
night for Hie sake of s eingher shadow on the
blind, have all been started off to Siberia.

The Ballot is the Thing.
The subjoined rough rhyme is now being circulated
up North among the voteless shearers :
Oh, comrades dear! and did your hear the news that’s
going round:
The Shearer is by law forbid to tent on camping-ground.
Unto the chain-gang’s clank again Australian woods shall
ring,
For they have found a law was made when George the
First was King.
It makes the Squatters sing, oh, it makes the
Squatters sing !
This vile old law that once was made when George
the First was King.
Wc used to have a notion that in Queensland men were
free,
That before the law the Squatter was the same as you or
me ;
But the sturdy Bushman now, they say, down to his knees
we’ll bring,
With this old law that Once was made when George the
First was King.
It makes the Squatters sing, oh, it makes tl e
Squatters sing !
This vile old law that once was made when George
the First was King.
And if you are a Railway-man I’d have you for to know
You’ve got to ask your gaffer where your sympathies
should go.
Your heart, your purse, your conscience to their keeping
you must bring—
Why, it’s even getting hotter than when George the First
was King!
When George the First was King, when George
the First was King ;
Why, it’s even getting hotter than when George
the First was King.
But keep your heads and tempers, boys. Your time will
come again,
Remember that your breath has made and can rramake
these men ;
When they’ve to face the ballot-box it’s mighty small
they’ll sing,
These men who’d drag us backward to the time when
George was King.
It’s mighty small they’ll sing, my hoys ; it’s
mighty small they’ll sing,
These men who’d drag us backwards to the time
when George was King.
Then keep your heads I say, my boys ; your comrades in
the town
Will help you yet to win a vote and put your tyrants
down.
Throw your old guns aside, my boys, the ballot is a thing
They did not have to reckon with when George the First
was King.
The ballot is the thing, my boys, the ballot is the
thing
Will show these men how long it is since George
the First was King.
Rockhampton, March 26.
W. KIDBTUN.
Mr. Clements Explains.
Many people have expressed surprise that Clem-
ents Tonic has met with such wonderful and un-
precedented success in such a short time. The
explanation is simple—it is genuine. I saw the
thousands of worthless hogswill American
nostrums flooding the Australian market year by
year, month by month, day by day, and knew that
a great amount of money left these shores annually
in return for these rubbishy articles, all of which
made the country so much the poorer. I also
knew that the exigencies of life in this country
and the climate necessitated the use of an artificial
bloodmaker to restore the normal condition and
vital forces which were so enervated by climatic
and other influences. I also saw the thin, pale,
delicate, and attenuated men and women of Aus-
tralian cities, and studied the causes and ways
and means of remedying the evil; I saw that it
was impossible to bring the article required into
popular favour except in the form of a patent
medicine. I knew of the thousands of pounds
spent by the public annually in purchasing inert
and sometimes harmful concoctions of worthless
ingredients, and asked myself —Why can’t we
manufacture the article the public require, and
make it of genuine and best materials, and give
an article which will do the good which other
makers only claim that theirs will do? I knew
that such a remedy could be made, and that unless
it met with public approval great loss must result.
However, I determined to venture on its introduc-
tion, and thus Clements Tonic came to be made,
and in the short space of two years it has spread
over the whole face of this continent. It is sold
in every store in Australasia as freely as in
Sydney : and the mere fact of its enormous sale
in this city is proof positive of its virtues, for no
article can command a great sale at the home of
its birth unless it is absolutely and conclusively
proved to be genuine. Clements Tonic has re-
placed every other article offered, and has the
largest consumption tenfold of any other Aus-
tralian medicine; and this is not due to the
advertising expenditure of its proprietor, but to
its pure, undisputed merit. Fellow Australians,
we don’t want the Yankee quack to dump his
ship-loads of clap-trap fooleries and cure-alls on
our Australian shores, and fool us with his
smooth tongue and plausible humbug ; neither do
we want him to suck the vitality and marrow out
of our people with his consignments of chemical
slops, which, with spacious plausibility, he
guarantees to cure everything from epilepsy to
impecuniosity. We want a genuine article, made
in our own land, by our own people, bought with
our own money ; then the money remains in our
own country, and the country is so much the
richer thereby, and we all have a chance of
handling it again; whereas, if it once gets into
the rapacious maw of the Yankee quack, farewell!
it is gone for ever. I know my article is genuine,
and that it contains the material to make it cure
disease where disease is curable. You may
depend upon it that if Clements Tonic fails, all
others must fail. I can.produce hundreds of
proofs of the truth of my statements and the
virtues of mv remedy. F. M. Clements, New-
town, N.S.W.
“It is worth its weight in gold,” is a common
expression. But, while the value of gold is easily
affected, the worth of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla, as a
blood-purifier, never depreciates. It eradicates
scrofula from the system.

Personal Items.
Parkes has his good points. For instance,
his nose isn’t red at the end.
Mr. O. E. Pilcher, Q.C., makes the seven-
teenth lawyer in the N.S.W. Legislative Council.
The title assumed by the eldest son of the
lately-ennobled Brewer Guinness is understood to
be Lord Pigbrand.
Parkes, should he not live long to avoid
it, will get 45 lines in Leslie Stephen’s “Dictionary
of National Biography.” Dr. Lang will have 50.
Colston, the self-confessed Narbethong
murderer, was a Soudan-contingent man. How-
ever, we don’t say so to prejudice his chance of
escaping the gallows.
Up to date only two speakers of the Vic-
torian Assembly, Lalor and Murphy, have died.
M‘Mahon and Duffy still flourish. Tolerably
Irish, this list of names.
General Sherman once said, concerning
his creed : “I believe in God Almighty—that’s as
far as I’ve got.” In other words, the General had
been successful up to date.
According to one of the daily papers, Lady
Jersey was “ the first to perceive that an accident
had happened at Middle Head. Some people
must grovel—even at a massacre.
The late Lord Granville seldom left his
hod before 2 p.m. A man like that will not find
the family vault so very irksome. It’s the busy
individual who objects to being buried.
Secretary Clibborn states that the widow
of the late Hon. James White has caused to bo
removed from the forfeit-list the names of all
persons indebted to the deceased’s estate.
The great Bradlaugh lived in shabby
apartments over a shop, and has left no estate but
a mass of law-books, into which he dug and
bored, with what results the world knows.
T. K. Pater, the eccentric Adelaide P.M.,
only allows twenty-five spectators in his court at
a time. This is a good hint to the semi-suffocated
minor-court officials of Sydney and Melbourne,
i It is common rumour in Perth that Sir
William Robinson is bidding farewell for ever to
Western Australia, and will resign his appoint-
ment as Governor as soon as he gets to London.
Two accounts reach us from America about
Peter Jackson, champion pugilist. One is that
Peter is drinking too much beer; the other that
he has joined the Salvation Army. Both are
probably true.
Not long ago died Williamson, Philadel-
phian millionaire. He had no house, or even a
lodging, but used to hire a doss every night, and
meal at restaurants. His knack was unrivalled
sharernarket manipulation.
General Grant, like Sir Henry Parkes,
did not like broad stories. Someone in a com-
pany, where he was, began by way of preface,
“I believe there are no ladies present.” “No,
said Grant; “but there are gentlemen !”
The Prince of Wales is undoubtedly a
great gambler and an expert at cards. The defunct
“ Joey ” Aylesford used to say with a comical
smile, “ Albert Edward does bar any chap who
takes liberties with the king—of trumps.”
Mr. C. W. Bloomfield, of the New South
Wales Government Printing Office, is advised by
English solicitors that he is heir to an English
baronetcy and a very large sum of money which
has been accumulating for the last sixty years.
David Syme was a contractor, ignorant of
journalism, when he joined his brother Ebenezer
in proprietorship of the Age. The latter’s death
cast the newspaper on David’s hands, when he
rose to the occasion. The Age has been 6d., 3d.,
2d., and Id.
The poet Wills, of Otahuhu, N.Z., has,
we read, had 1113 poems printed, and he has over
700 further effusions on hand that have not yet
seen the light. It is nearly forty years since the
Family Herald printed his first song—“ There’s
Love in Her Merry Blue Eye. ”
A certain Queensland Mayor, now very
wealthy through a gold-grab, has resigned because
he refuses to sit at the same Council-table as a
coloured man. When this haughty mayor mended
watches in Sydney a few years back, he was glad
enough to take a darkey’s little half-crown.
Dr. Koch is said to be much depressed by
his failure to cure consumption of the lungs in
one act. Some men take these things to heart,
whilst others preserve a cheerful demeanour
under the most adverse circumstances. We never
hear of “Elijah” Egeson drowning himself in
the floods.

lyn, was Herbert, son-in-law of the recently-
deceased Sir Francis Murphy. His brother Henry
went to England, leaving the £90,000, or so, in
their partnership, but Mortimer nobbled the lot
for the World and Federal Australian, and
has the impudence to claim the authorship
of the “Commonwealth.”

£20,000 a day. And as he then will be grabbing
ono subsistence—;according to English rates—of
292,000 persons, there will be exactly 292,000
reasons why the said Duke should be hanged out
of the road without ceremony.

Harold Mallalieu, the young Nyngan
homicide, who claims that he slew his victim in
hot blood and in self-defence as the outcome of a
dispute on the labour question, went under the
alias of “Michael Black,” in a vain endeavour,
perhaps, to escape the bad fortune imposed upon
him by the person responsible for christening
him “Harold.” All Harolds have been singularly
unlucky—from the Saxon Harold downwards.
Inseparably enwreathed with the new
eight hours’ memorial, Melbourne, must be the
name of James Galloway, the founder. He died
at 32, and Ids monument is in the Melbourne
cemetery. But his absolute claim, as single
originator, is disputed, though the stonemasons,
to whom he belonged, admittedly have the glory.
The old, well-known banner of 35 years ago is still
carried at the head of the annual eight-hour pro-
cession.
One of Melbourne’s good women is the
appropriately-named Widow Bon, friend of the
Aborigines, and referred to by the officialdom of
the past as “a most pestiferous woman,” but she
gained the victory, with Graham Berry’s assist-
ance as Chief (Secretary. Between them they
settled the point that no aboriginal on Ooranderrk
Station, or elsewhere, is to be made to work. Mrs.
Bon, being a rich squatteress, could never be
snuffed out.

else,” is absurd, in face of what is widely known
about Booth’s history.

Hon. John William Fortescue, son of Earl
Fortescue, and aide-de-camp to Sir W. Jervois
when N.Z. Governor, has been writing in Nine-
teenth Century on “ The Seamy Side of
Australia.” A Sunday paper;—At a N.Z.
country hotel a bush bummer asked him to shout.
“Do you know who I am?” demanded the irate
aide-de-camp; “I am the hon. Mr. Fortescue.”
“ Don’t care a d if you’re the dishonourable
Mr. Fiftyscue,” replied the unabashed thirsty
one, “I want a long ’un; are you going to
shout?” Mr. Fortescue did “shout,” and now,
adds our Sunday contemporary, he is shouting
again.
Mr. Justice Windeyer, as the oldest
graduate of the Sydney University, was loudly
cheered at the University crews' dinner on Satur-
day evening. The Justice has quite survived Sir
William (then Mister) Maeleay’s memorable jibe
uttered many years ago during a debate in the
New South Wales Assembly on something con-
nected with the finance of Sydney University, of
which institution Mr. Windeyer was then the
only graduate ; —“ The State has expended so
many”—we forget how many—“thousands, of
pounds,” said Mr. Macleay, “and that’’-point-
ing to Mr. Windeyer, who sat opposite—“is all
we’ve got for it.”

Dibbs’ motion that the Federal capital
shall be at Sydney certainly looks like a torpedo.
“Such things send the Crown to the
melting-pot. —London Star on Gordon-Cumming’s
case.
Sir Patrick Jennings thinks that the elec-
toral systems of the colony are “ sufficiently
liberal.”
Tasmania warms up on the Capital ques-
tion. Why not Hobart ? The Straits form a
fatal objection.
Dr. Garranhas a powerful and irrefragable
article, in the “ Australian Year Book,” 1891, on
the failure of Denominational education.
Alfred Deakin has refused to accept the
Chief Secretaryship of Victoria under Munro,
“ for private reasons.” Age before honesty ?
The Queensland champions of “freedom
of contract” are agitating to induce the Govern-
ment to import batches of cheap Italian laborers —
vendettas and all.
TheN.S.W. alleged Treasury surplus looks
at present like a bookkeeper’s surplus. Some of
the Treasurer’s critics say that N.S.W. has a
deficiency, just now, of £4OOO a day.
The Convention delegates, having got over
their reverence for Parkes’ hair, loudly express
their wonder that so apparently shallow a person
should have had “ so long a run ” in N.S. Wales
Premier Parkes literally squirmed in his
seat at the Federal Convention on hearing Sir
George Grey’s notice of motion that the Constitu-
tion should be submitted to a plebiscite of the
Australian people.
“Freedom of contract,” as it is under-
stood in Australia is inconsistent with the con-
tinuance of unionism, declares B. R. Wise —who,
by the Way, says he is not likely to bo in a position
to re-enter public life for some yeai’s.

have advanced excellent reasons against the adop
tion of Convention-delegate Wrixon's proposal
that a man once convicted of felony should be
disqualified for election to the Federal Parlia-
ment.
Since 1815 the Rothschilds have raised for
Great Britain more than 250 millions, for Austria
60 millions, for Prussia 50 millions, for France 100
millions, for Italy 75 millions, for Russia 30
millions, for Brazil 15 millions, and- so on in a
descending scale.
One of the rocks which will assist in the
splitting up of the Liberal-Conservative Ministry
of Western Australia will be Chief-Justice On-
slow’s return to office. Premier Forrest will lead
the van in the restoration of the Judge to his
former position.

oven “Realm” would indicate separation, for
“Australian Realm” would simply imply Aus-
tralian sovereignty.

Some instructive ideas on the value of
capital-punishment are derivable from the New
Orleans vendetta episode. In Sicily to kill a man
meant —and still often means—certain death to
the murderer at the hands of the victim’s relatives
afterwards. Did this act as a deterrent to murder?
History says NO in a very loud tone of voice.

ton on the colonies :—
Lord Oarington discoursed last Monday on Australia in
the presence of the Prince of Wales, and said, in effect,
that if we assist the Australians to accomplish all their
aims, and sanctify whatever means they take to gain
them, they will be uncommonly loyal. Very like, very
like,
When Grey, in Convention, declared that
the Queen could disallow any and every act of a
colonial Parliament, he was met by a chorus of
dissent. The old man boldly and truly told the
dissentients “ they did not know what they were
talking about, and that they were ignorant of the
Constitution.”
The mean mortality among the well-to-do
at most is one in sixty. At this proportion of
mortality the average deaths in Europe should not
exceed five millions. Yet, the death-rate is 15
That is to say, that the cold, hunger,
immorality, disease and militarism that spring
from monopoly kill ten millions of people annually
on one continent alone.
Disgraceful disloyalty in Melbourne Trades
Hall Council, the secretary singing Ri-tooral-
looral amidst a royalist speech. The occasion was
a shameful debate on whether to invite Governor
Hopetoun (at the expense of £70 for an address
and fizz) to be present at the eight hours’ fete.
If he goes after this his pachyderm must be as
thick as that of a rhinoceros.
A Newcastle (Eng.) paper states that
the wooden-looking and uproarious Patterson is
bound to be Premier of Victoria at a very early
date. And, strangely enough, Patterson was in
Newcastle at the time, and had been interviewing
the editor. It is a very small individual indeed
who can’t pass himself off as a coming Premier
when he is 16,000 miles away from the people who
would see him boiled first.
Sir Arthur Hunter Palmer, K.C. M.G.,
President of the Legislative Council of Queens-
land, and Acting-Governor of that province, ap-
peared before a Land Court in Brisbane the other
day and “ridiculed the idea” that a Dividing
Commissioner could have ridden over one of his
runs “in 18 days, travelling at the rate of 35
miles per day.” Palmer’s class also ridicule the
idea that landless working men have any real
grievance.
Brewer Fitzgerald, of Victoria, who, by
the way, is newly-related to the British nobility
by marriage, characterised Doctor Cockburn’s
proposal that the basis of the Commonwealth
should be one-man-one-vote “as a pure piece of
impudence.” Apropos, South Australia, and New
Zealand are now on a one-man-one-vote basis, and
Victoria, South Australia and Queensland are
committed to the same thing, so far as their
existing Governments go.
If the Labour party—which represents a
large majority of the vox populi —wishes to rule
and upset the reign of a minority, who hold sway
simply by splitting the majority into sections,
the cardinal principle to be observed must be
that, in future, on’y one issue shall he fought out at
a time at the ballot-box. The classes skilfully pre-
vent the domination of the masses by introducing
conflicting side issues, when a direct vote is
required on the subject of social reconstruction.
Just look at these two preambles. This :
Wo, the people of the United States, in order to form a
more perfect union, establish justice, insure domestic
tranquility, provide for the common defence, promote
the general welfare, and secure the blessings of liberty to
ourselves and our posterity, do ordain and establish ‘this
Constitution for the United States of America.
And this :
Whereas the Australasian Colonies of ... . have
. . . . agreed to unite in one Federal Common-
wealth under the Crown under the United Kingdom of
Great Britain and Ireland, and under the Constitution
hereby established. r?
A difference in the ring, isn’t there?
Dear Bulletin, —This is how public
opinion goes in the country. A cricket-match was
being played, the other day, at a place called
Cookardihia, about midway been Wagga and
\lbury. After the match, when the opposing
teams met at the local pub. and the usual toasts
of “ Visitors,” “ Captains,” “ Umpires and
Scorers,” &c., had been got through, the captain of
the visiting team got up and proposed the toast of
“The Coming Australian Republic.” The
enthusiastic way in which that toast was received
would have turned Government House green.
There were about 40 present, and not one man in
the room refrained from cheering ! —Scotty.
Two extracts from same issue of Sydney
D.T., from same set of telegrams and referring to
same subject:—
A railway porter at Baroaldine, who refused to be sworn
in as a special constable, has been dismissed. In all, seven
railway employes have been dismissed for aiding and
abetting the strikers.
Three magistrates were travelling along the Central
line to-day swearing in as special constables all the rail-
way employes who were willing to be sworn.
A S.M. Herald telegram of same date reads :—
None of the men offered any objection to being sworn
in.
Of course, they didn’t.
At the Convention :—
Sir George Grey contended that the right of the Con-
vention was to make this federation a perfect machine,
lie denied that this principle of one man one vote should
be left for others to get for them.
Mr. Baker: Is New Zealand intending to join the
federation ?
Sir Georgs Grey: New Zealand wishes to have the
power to do so, and wishes, therefore, to enter into a
commonwealth of which it approves. lie asked why this
right had been kept from the people so long?
Members; Who kept it?
Sir George Grey: Why evade a debate by a question
like that ? lion, members all knew who had withheld it—
the legislatures, which did not properly represent the
people.
The Federal Convention cannot safeguard
the Commonwealth in the matter of defence, for
this reason—the Governor-General, if appointed
by the Crown, is Commander-in-Chief by virtue
of his commission. , The Convention cannot
delimit his powers, because the act of delimita-
te n would ho.ultra vires. The Cabinet Ministry
of the Commonwealth might decline to dispose of
or even call out tiro Federal military forces, but
yet, according to law, the Governor-General
would have the authority to do so, and any officer
or man refusing to obey the Commander-in-Chief’s
order would he mutinous. The military and naval
forces of the Commonwealth will not be under the
control of t'm Commonwealth’s Parliament. The
Parliament’s only check on the abuse of the mili-
tary power will be that of supplies.

'of Representatives was reached. The point
’was whether the Federal Government of
’Australia should or should not be consti-
tuted on the basis of manhood suffrage, and
the vote of the Convention was to the effect
that it should not. The provision made for
electing the House of Representatives is that
each state shall return its quota of members
by the suffrages of only such persons as are
entitled to vote for its local Parliament.'
The impression which has hitherto pre-
vailed, that there Was to be one House to
represent the states of Australia and
another to represent the people of
[turns out, therefore, to be a delusion. The
I states as states are to be represented in both
[ Houses and the people as people in none.’'

The Bulletin tenders its sympathy to
Henniker Heaton on the ground that he has lost
the chance of re-enacting the part of Beaconsfield.
Why did he leave it to The Bulletin to suggest—
with its tongue in its cheek —that Her Most
Gracious should be given a new title—“ Queen of
Australia ” with a view of clinching, so to speak,
the loyalty of her loving antipodean subjects. If
Henniker had only struck the idea, he might have
been made a duke,

The worst of such notoriety as Melba’s is
that she gets so talked about, and honest (sorry
we can’t say poor-r but honest) Pere Dave Mitchell
wishes to Heaven she had stopped at Yarraberg,
and never had the Presbyterian Ladies’ College
putting operatic cranks into her cranium. Let a
woman be ever so good and she shall not escape
calumny on the stage. Melba’s detractors have
numbered a Prince to commence with among her
slain. Usually it is the chief tenor who is hinted at
—the three-chinned Nicolini sort of chap—fellow
who pours salad oil over his Irish stew wan.

The Dook of Manchester’s visit to Aus-
tralia reminds us of the famous epitaph on a
baby’s tombstone :
Brief was his sd*.> .
Ie just looked around,
d then went away.
Bo far as Meluourne and Sydney are concerno. ,
he didn’t get one banquet, and nobody asked

allowed him to come and go without troubling to
find out whether his Grace had been favourably
-mpressed by what he saw. Why, we don’t even

» * *
W hen the first accounts of the sad. disaster
at Middle Head reached the public, many people
were under the impression that the fatal mine
w vs exploded by Lady .Terser.
r our luon —joieurc.iaiits Hamuiand and
Bedford, Corporal M‘Kee, R.E., and Bugler
Charles Bennett—were blown to atoms by°the
explosion. They were engaged with eight others
in a boat in laying guncotton mines to replace
two which had been fired by Lady Jersey earlier
in the day. Of the twelve men, but two escaped
uninjured. The party had laid one mine and,
'Having rowed to some distance, ventured to
explode it. However, “the cables must have
been confused,” and the guncotton, still slung to
the boat-side, was fired instead of the mine beneath
the water. The bodies of the dead were simply
blown to rags, and Lieutenant Hammand’s wife
witnessed her husband’s death from the shore, as
did also several hundreds of civilian spectators.
The military court of inquiry sat in the
evening with closed doors. " Colonel Do
Wolski, head of the N.S. Wales military
engineers, when interviewed that evening, said
it was absurd to have such demonstrations, not
for the benefit of the soldiers, but for the amuse-
ment of the public and officials. The pastime, he
remarked, was an altogether illegitimate one :
The banqueting and junketing in consequence of this
business spells ruin to solid work. There was bungling
and those in the boat evidently got hold of the wrong
end of the cable. . So long as the men fire these
hasty charges merely for the purposes of a picnic, merely
for the purposes of a public demonstration, you must ex-
pect the possibilities of accident. The fact is, we ought
not to do anything which is not in the service. We should
not do all this and run these risks merely to give ladies
a fine, pleasant afternoon.
Now, what does all this mean? We do not know
what Colonel De Wolski intends it to signify, but
the general public, which has heard so much of,
if it has not actual personally experienced, the
copious libations attendant on Australian soldier-
ing, has jumped to a certain conclusion concerning
the cause of this lamentable disaster. It is much
to be regretted that it has been decided to hold no
inquest or public enquiry.
* * *
One of the most exciting incidents yet
recorded in connection with New South
Wales militarism occurred some years ago.
An officer, by profession a schoolmaster,
and therefore doubly a martinet, loved to look
upon the rum when it was red, and was in
the habit, when ids battery of artillery were on
the roads, of pulling them up outside public-
houses while he went in to give himself a drink.
Late one afternoon he and his gallant follower 8
passed through Waverley, where their commande r
gave the usual “ Halt!” for the usual reason, but
the battery had been pulled up often enough that
day—they were tired and wanted to get home, so
they took no notice: whereupon the gallant officer
rode along and, brandishing his huge cavalry-
sword at the throat of the driver of the first gun,
said: “ Wretch, stop or die !” Th wretch turned
white and stopped, whereupon Captain B. went
in and said he thought he really would have a
little more rum.
* * *
Captain B.’s battery, by the way, con-
tained at that time a very dainty-looking little
man with a Jersey-coloured beard, who had
mincing, purring little ways of his own, and
actually took with him to the annual camp a pair
of kid boots and a frilled nightshirt. Before the
troops had been in camp half-a-week it was dis-
covered that the pretty little man with the frilled
nightgown was the hangman’s assistant. Never
judge a man by his nightgown.

Although on the Statute-book, capital
punishment is virtually abolished in South Aus-
tralia. The S.A. Government admit that the
prisoner Lowenthal’s sentence was commuted
owing to their repugnance of the death-penalty.
Judgeßoucaut, administrator in Kintore’s absence,
holds the same views, and has never yet been a
party to a death-sentence.
* * *
If you want final and. convincing proof
that the Englishman is the noblest work of the
Creator, the place to look for it is at the Mildura
(Yic.) irrigation-colony. The population for tbe
greater part consists of the class from which the
jackeroo used to be recruited. Every ship brings
out a draft of English youngsters who have been
too good, or too bad, or too stupid to earn their
living in the dear old country, and have been
packed off to the swell settlement to save trouble.
The place is essentially English, and on a Satur-
day afternoon (following the arrival of the Eng-
lish mail with the remittances) you will see more
Piccadilly panoply and more scientific cane-suck-
ing than on a spring afternoon in Collins-street.
There is a considerable proportion of duffers
among them, but a few give promise of growing
into men. It is worth noting that “colonials”
hold nearly all the responsible positions. The
head accountant, the bank-manager, the school-
master, the lawyer, the two editors, one of the
doctors, the four surveyors, the auctioneers, the
four architects, and the station manager are all
Australians. In fact, wherever brains, energy,
and executive ability are essentials, that is where
someone other than an Englishman has to be ap-
pointed. The water-inaster is a Swede, the head
planter is a Swiss, and the majority of the other
bosses are either Canadian or Australian. Which
shows that the British lion has a tail.
A few nights ago the adherents of Mildura
Anglican Church met to hold a pow-wow about
the subject of building a synagogue. It was
suggested that an architect should be requested to
design an elevation to be examined at a future
meeting. One of the devout present—a most
exaggerated masher, recently imported from the
city of four-storey collars and balloon-pants—was
suddenly attacked with an idea which, among a
wilderness of haws and lisps, found expression in
a suggestion that the man should be instructed to
“ pwovide for ventilation ath well ath elevation.”
The beastly colonials cacchinated promptly and
violently, and the presiding parson, after an
honest attempt to assume a pained and sepulchral
aspect, had to let go and join. There was a great
awakening, and serious business had to be sus-
pended. It is even betting that the Belgravian
product doesn’t see it yet.

The volatile Sir William Robinson intends
tearing himself away from W.A. on a three-
months’ leave of absence, and it is believed he will
not return from England, when ho gets there.
Lady Robinson remains at Perth for the present,
which strikes one as a strange reversal of the usual
order of things :
“ Observant but Illiterate ” is a remarkable
letter in a Melbourne paper, a few days back.
The writer lately got work as hospital-attendant,
and comments on the medical causes of death,
“ bronchitis,” and so on, which, he says, should in
two-thirds of the cases be written down as simply
Poverty.

The cable gave us, the other day, a simple
word-picture of the Nitrate King and Court con-
fidentially proclaiming from the housetops their
intention to buy Tasmanian silver-mine shares at
an early date. If Colonel North has indeed
noised his project abroad it is Lombard-street to a

and feels a strange yearning to unload ’em. The
cable-yarn reads altogether too much like a North
windfall for this deserving young country.
# « *
Bush residents have at times to put up
with queer medical attendance. The other day a
doctor in a N.S.W. Southern town, having cadged
many drinks on the ground of illness, went
round the local hotels —there were nine—and had
a second nip at each. Later in the day he was at
the blacksmith’s when a newly-married couple
went by. “Look here, doctor,” said the town
wag, “if you’ll go after those two on all-fours
and bark like a dog I’ll shout for you.” “Shout
for me? Will you? Well, here goes.” And
down he went on all-fours and followed behind
the blushing pair with his “ Bow-wow-wow ” all
the way to the first public-house, where he waited
for and duly got his drink.
# * #
Klock Yung Gee, of Ballarat (Vic.), applied
for a divorce the other day, and prepared the case
himself on a dirty piece of brown paper, with the
corner eaten off and a grease-mark in the centre.
The statement ran thus : “Too muchey talkee
with neighbours, too muchey paint face, too
muchey dlink Yulopean blandy, too muchey fight,
too muchey snore in sleep, too muchey boss, too
muchey say killum husband, too muchey no good,
too muchey run away with nigger.”

# # *
“ Dagonct,” of London Referee, sizes up
the lately-deceased Duke of Somerset: —
He was the second oldest duke in the kingdom. He
was a harmless old gentleman who came to live in
Brighton two or three months ago, and was not at all the
sort of person one would have suspected of being a duke,
unless one had been told that a duke was living in the
house he came out of or went into. Even then the
curious in such matters might have mistaken the other
gentleman, the one who opened the door and surveyed
outside mankind with such chilling indifference, for the
representative of all the Seymours. I have seen several
gentlemen all staying at the same time in the cheap
boarding-house round the corner, any one of whom was
far more like the conventional idea cf an English duke
than was the Duke of Somerset.
Which merely goes to show that the conventional
idea of a duke is dreadfully wrong. There is still
a certain hazy impression floating about that a
duke is a tali and stately individual who is always
throwing up entrenchments somewhere, or cast-
ing off his allegiance, or charging wildly upon the
enemy; whereas he is generally a little, fuzzy,
muddle-headed old man who dodders about on
two uncertain legs, with Ids pants turned up at
the feet, and trails a big umbrella behind him like
an addled devil dragging his weary tail along the
gutter. And the lofty individual with the noble
features that theworld takes for a duke is the assist-
ant at a second-rate barber's shop, and the only
thing he does with his kingly countenance is to
open it when he asks if you will have your hair
short at the back.
# * *
“Billy” Heffernan, who died the other
day at Dunedin (Maoriland), has left a void
among the old identities of Bendigo, for that
golden city will never grow another individual
like him. He was of a vast and unfathomable
age, and was proud of his antiquity, and as a rule
he tacked on a year to his record every month or
six weeks, so that his friends could almost hear
him growing old. When he died he must have
counted about 250 years. While he was still an
unsophisticated child of 125 or so he made his pile
and lost it again ; he was a big shareholder in the
Great Extended Hustlers mine at a time when
that delusive claim seemed to be a solid hunk of
gold, and he suffered from a conviction that it
was going to last for ever. Then there came a
sudden end to the boom, and Heffernan was left
with SOOlbs of unsaleable scrip in every wildcat
mine around Bendigo, and a big mortgage on his
famous Shamrock Hotel. Ho consoled him-
self for the loss of his fortune by growing old
more violently than ever, and his favourite amuse-
ment was to sit on the capacious balcony of the
Shamrock and listen to his years accumulating.
He was an authority on all raining matters, and
by his valuable advice he assisted the writer of
this paragraph to get into a complicated specula-
tion in which he might have made £lO, and
couldn’t lose more than £6OO. Having lost the
£6OO, the said writer lowered his bag out of the
window by a string and fled on foot to Kangaroo
BY THE BRINY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Sullen the surge of the old, old sea
On which the old, old moon was beaming,
And the old, old tide crept stealthily
To where the lovers sat a-dreaming.
Some explanation this demands—
The twain were squatting on the sands.
And he was young and she was fair
(The old, old thing in humdrum verses.)
The sea-spray kissed the slumbrous air,
The seagull croaked his mystic curses.
When little rhymes like this we spin
We always work the seagull in.
Their hearts beat time to the olden tune,
The tune that Eve brought into fashion ;
And Love’s own self caught up the rune,
And every breeze was hot with passion.
“ Rune ” always in the third verse goes,
But what it means—well, goodness knows.
The old, old darkness came at last
To fling its cloak round forms that shivered,
They sat and sat like ghosts aghast,
And quaked the while the light’ning quivered.
You’ll not deny, of course (who could ?)
That “ ghosts aghast ” is rather good.
The red sun rose in the old, old way,
The seagull cursed more loud and shrilly,
As the old, old morn broke cold and grey
On man and maid, who’d both gone silly.
Thfl fact, perchance, you have discerned—
’Twas I, the bard, whose head was turned.
E.F.
Troubadour vice-regal,
Soaring like an eagle
In the realms of fancy,
And of music, and of song,
We would know the reason
(If it isn’t treason)
Why you are vamoosing—
Tell us, Billy, what is wrong ?
Giddy great composer,
Is the cause sub rosa
Why you spread your pinions,
And to colder regions fly ?
Come behind the curtain—
Let us know for certain —
Won’t you tell us why, Robin,
Won’t you tell us why ?

* * #
The proprietor of Pearson’s Weekly
lately offered a “ prize of a wife and a hundred a
year” for competition among bachelor subscribers.
After selecting from among a van-load of photos,
and testimonials several eligible ladies for inspec-
tion, carriage paid, they (Pearson and his wife)
picked cut a plum. Then came a bitch. Hun-
dreds of would-be husbands (Woman’s World tells
the tale) had sent in their names—retired officers
of both services, ex-Indian civilians, and a decided
preponderance of parsons who are always
“there” on such occasions. A certain number of
gentlemen wore selected —were introduced to the
young lady, and entertained at tea by her mother
in one of the suburbs of London. Pints of tea
and pounds of cake were thus consumed. Still
the suits came; but the right man came not, and
seemed never likely to come. After weeks of
interviewing and rejecting by the chosen lady,
and an expenditure of large sums in travelling-
expenses, Mr. Pearson was obliged to withdraw
the offer and admit that his novel form of matri-
monial agency had proved a failure. Now he
declares that his only mistake lay in selecting a
woman “so excellent, so sensible, and self-
respecting that no husband is good enough for
her. ”
* * #
The two Mormon elders who turned up
at Towoomba (Q.) the other day and managed to
gather an audience of 100 at the local Masonic
Hall, were dismal young men who snuffled texts
and talked dreary solemnities for over an hour,
but never told the bright gathering of expectant
girls who formed a big blushing contingent of the
meeting a word about love, or courtship, or mar-
riage, or harems, or anything that makes this
wicked world sinfully endurable. Even the M.P.
present voted the show a frost, and long before
the Saints had got half through, all but a half-
dozen or so had adjourned to carry out their own
notions of religious exercises adown the moonlit
paths afar from the township’s shadows. Every
Mormon missionary is a hamstrung cripple ever
since the Church in Utah abolished polygamy for
veiled concubinage, and his cross is hard to bear.

How much will the the Premier Permanent
depositors get ? A capitalist bought off a lot in
the first scare at 12s. Gd. per £l, and it is believed
he will make a big haul. However, such discount
soon choked off depositors, and there is an idea
that the sweating of the contributories, by the
industrious lemon-squeezing Templeton, will foot
up over 20s. in the £.

The curent forgeries on the Commercial
Bank of Australia are so excellent that many
bankers have been taken in ; notes representing
no less than £l3O last week found their way into
one bank’s head-office from its branches. It is
believed that the forgers have successfully traded
off thousands of notes.

It is recorded in the papers that a N.Z.
man who had had his leg amputated under
chloroform calmly smoked his pipe three-quarters
of an hour after the operation. As an example of
pluck this altogether pales before the case (several
years ago) of one Hourigan, of Western Victoria,
who, having had both arms dreadfully crushed in
a machine, declined to take chloroform, and
smoked his pipe while both limbs were being
amputated by a surgeon.

Some London papers laugh at the notion
that “Bill” Gumming was bounced into signing a
confession of cheating. They say that William
loves a row, that battle and fighting are to him as
the very breatli of his own nostrils, that it is al-
most incredible that any male being, no matter
how exalted, could have made any insinuation
against him without being thrashed ; and the man
or woman who can frighten William into doing
anything in any way repugnant to his feelings or
harmful to his interests has yet to be born.
Apropos, when the beautiful Miss Minnie
Stephens, the American millionairess, first arrived
in England she was eagaged to be married to one
of the Marlborough H«use set, who was caught
red-handed cheating at cards at Nice. Gordon-
Cumming, who is poor for his social status, tried
hard to marry the rich Yankee, Miss Leiter, but
he failed to catch her even with the aid of
“ Wales.”

The question (says a London press corres-
pondent) is fiercely disputed as to who it was that
violated the compact of secrecy in regard to the
Cordon-Gumming scandal—
The story which survives all others in authority is that
the day after the exposure “Wales” drove over to tea
with a certain lady, to whom he confided the facts, she in
turn confiding them some time afterwards to her bosom
friend, a youthful peeress. The latter was adored by a
certain baronet, familiarly known as “Topps,” and some
weeks later Gordon-Cumming, who has a sarcastic tongue,
happening to see the pair together, jokingly observed to
the lady, “When your husband’s dead I hope Topps will
make an honest woman of you.” He little knew that his
silly jest was a home-thrust, and would he resented
accordingly. But resented it was, the intensely indig-
nant lady taking her revenge in the form of a publication
to all and sundry of the Tranby-Croffc scandal.
The Bulletin doesn’t publish this so much as an
item of news, as for the purpose of illustrating the
morals of high society as indicated by its conver-
sation. Lord Ronald Gower is understood to be
the Australasian’s correspondent.

A Victorian hospital recently advertised
for contributions of literature for the use of the
patients, There was only one response. The
most ungrammatical alderman in the district sent
in all his speeches for the last seven years or so,
and now the convalescents are all sitting up and
reading things presumably like these: “Mr.
Mayor. Sorr ! I gets hup to state as the party
that spoke afore_ don’t know nothink. Wot this
’ere town wants is a bloomin’ hoverdraft. ”
* * *
A Melbourne scribe describes that indes-
cribable dance—the “larrikin waltz”; “ One of
the sights of popular demonstration days is the
larrikin waltz. On the one side you have an
aggregation of undersized, loathsome - looking
weeds in high-heeled boots, gaudy-coloured neck-
ties, and bell-bottomed trousers; on the other a
crowd of dwarfed factory-girls with faces so hard
that you could strike sparks from them almost.
They tangle themselves together in some remark-
able fashion, and gyrate. The young lady keeps
her head well on one side, not so much from
maiden modesty as to get out of range of the
young man’s breath. By the evening they are all
more or less drunk, and you scarcely wonder that
a social reformer, coming across such a scene,
elects to give mankind up in despair and let it
go to perdition in its own way.”
One of the things which Her Gracious will
not endure is to have any married woman about
her who wears her hair hanging down her back.
Consequently, ladies attached to the Imperial
Court have to keep their thatch in a knob, or else
clear out. There is a legend that one lady in-
waiting,” or “ woman of the bedchamber, or
“ maid-of-honour,” or some similar official who
held an appointment about the royal establish-
ment, appeared with her golden locks streaming
over her shoulders, and when she was remonstrated
with, she seized her wet umbrella, which had been
standing up against the throne to drip, and said
emphatically: “Victoria, you’re too confoundedly
particular. This ain’t your hair.” Then she
crowded her goloshes into her bag, looked sternly
at the royal countenance through her green spec-
tacles, and grasping her trunk and her port-
manteau disappeared down the stair. And as she
disappeared the Queen, in a voice stifled with
indignation, instructed the Equerry of the Cold
Meat Department to hang on like mad to the bell,
and inform the First Lord of the Coal and Wood
to tell the Baron at the front door that if that
awful woman called again Her Majesty was out,
and she wasn’t to be allowed to wait. i bis
anecdote is probably untrue, seeing that we in-
vented it ourselves, but it is as thrilling and as
reliable as royal anecdotes in general.
* * *
The correct thing now in fashionable
circles in America is for a girl to present her
future husband with plaster-oasts of her hands
and feet. It is needless to mention that, so far as
the feet are concerned, these images are a hollow
lie and an imposture, for the fashionable girl is
not yet born wh) would deliberately present her
betrothed with a statue of her corns and bunions.
The oasts all show five perfectly-formed toes, and
a daintily-arched instep, and a skin as smooth as
polished ivory. But sometimes a dubious lover
takes the plaster hoof for a reality, and marries
on the strength of it; and when he finds that th
genuine extremity is large and cold, and horribl
on a winter night, lie realises that he has bee •>
cold. Then he skirmishes for a divorce on thn
ground that the lady’s feet weren’t up to sample.
* * *
Early last week, when the shearing row
was suffering a temporary lull, and there was no
news from the Federal Convention, and no ’orrid
murder, and everything was stagnant, the daily
papers had to fill up with the old, old cablegram
about Russia “massing troops on the Austrian
frontier.” About four years ago that diseased
assertion used to appear in print almost daily,
but it has dropped out of sight recently, and it
was a shock to see the enamelled and painted face
of the familiar, wrinkled lie glaring out of the
same old column.

The residents at Finnigan’s, a North
Queensland township way back from Cooktown,
just before “Patrick’s Day” offered £3O reward
ito the enterprising bullocky who would first
deliver a cask of grog there. In view of this
ghastly fact, how futile are the endeavours of
teetotal reformers !

An American judge says the man who
would not perjure himself to save a woman cannot
be believed on his oath on any other subject.
Dicey’s “Law of the Constitution” says
that “ Bradlaugh re-discovered the law of main-
tenance ; elucidated the law of blasphemy; ex-
plained the character of a penal action ; enabled
us to define with precision the relation between
the House of Commons and the courts of the
land; has gone far to make intelligible the
legal character and solemnity of an oath._ His
failure or success are from this point of view at
least equally advantageous to the nation, and
will, one may reflect with satisfaction, equally
ensure to him his appropriate reward. He will
obtain, or rather he has obtained legal immortality
Everywhere there are signs of royalty dying
out: The United States and Mexico, solid Re-
publics ; collapse of opposition to French Republic;
stability in Republics of South America, includ-
ing Brazil; Portugal looming up, as necessary
sequence. Only by clerical political dominance
can the Republic be choked, for the time, in Bel-
gium. Spain follows Portugal. French influence
cannot be ultimately resisted in Spain and Italy.
Infant monarchs in Holland, Spain, Servia, and
the Belgian and Austrian succession both spoilt.
Russia weltering. At least two-fifths of the
British population openly Republican. Germany
evidently on the verge of a terrible crisis, through
the Emperor’s fear of the Socialists alienating
from him successively Bismarck, Waldersee, and
Caprivi. Bismarck going into Parliament on the
suffrage—an indisputable sign of Democracy’s
march. Impossibility of fixing up the Turkish
peninsula otherwise than as a Republic. Move-
ment even in India. Canada and Newfoundland
yearning for the United States. Lastly, Aus-
tralia’s “ Commonwealth.”
When the late Jack Macrossan, M.L.A.,
was digging in Ravenswood and his “ hatter’s ”
claim didn’t turn out well, he struck a new line as
a general scrivener—many and many a love-letter
came from his pen—for illiterate diggers. His
“office’’was a flimsy “ put-up,” but it gathered
him in much coin, and, what was more valuable
still, procured for him an intimate “ family friend-
ship” with hundreds. Ravenswood was then a
vivid green, as regards the majority of its popula-
tion, and the pick-handed Irish prospector whose
fist was awkward at the pen, was Macrcssan’s
most numerous and regular client, every monthly-
mail time, when it was fashionable “to sind a bit
uv a wroiting and a pound or two ” to the “ ould
sod.” Those were the days when miners had
voting-papers to enable them to exercise their
electoral franchise. Before an election, organisa-
tions of diggers—one Orange, t’other Green—used
to send out forage-parties whose mission was to
bounce or steal voting-tickets and record them at
the polling-booths. In 1873, Jack Macrossan was
returned to Parliament, and, once in, he managed
to stick.
Why a Panic?
A leading financier was recently asked, “ what
makes a financial panic ?” He tersely replied :
“It is simply a lack of confidence.” The com-
merce of the world is the creature of confidence.
One-tenth, perhaps, of large business transactions
arc made in currency ; the other nine-tenths are
accomplished through the medium of personal
cheques —mere pledges of confidence. Business
would come to a standstill if currency was generally
demanded. There isn’t enough of it to go round.
A panic arises when men, losing confidence,
demand currency instead of cheques, the currency
cannot be had—-there isn’t enough of it, and the
banks lock up what there is. Confidence is the
basis of commercial prosperity. The most in-
telligent people bank the heaviest on confidence,
and prosperity follows. The singular thing is that
sometimes, men who prosper in thus placing con-
fidence in their fellows in business transactions
act as though in other matters of even greater
moment, no reliance could be placed in them. If
you believe what straightforward business men
say, there need be no panic about kidney disease.
It is curable. Mr. N. S. Parmintier, Warehouse-
man, writes : Glen Osmond, Adelaide, l9th
November, 1890. —Ever since my arrival in the
colony, now some four years since, I have suffered
from an aggravated liver complaint, combined
with a serious affection of the kidneys. After
the use of four bottles of Warner’s safe cure, I had
gained weight, and my pains materially subsided,
and when I had taken 12 bottles all symptoms of
kidney disease were effectually removed. The
Mayor of Albany, West Australia, Robert Muir,
Esq., says:— For a considerable time I have suf-
fered from a disorder of the kidneys, together
with a number of other distressing symptoms.
With the use of three bottles of Warner’s Safe
Cure every trace of kidney disease was removed.
Writes Mr. Arthur J. Diamond, manager for
Messrs. Tolley & Co., Limited, of Ereemantle,
W.A., 11th December. 1890.—For two or three
years I have on and off suffered from an affection
of tin kidneys. I have taken, as occasion re-
quired it, about twelve bottles of Warner’s safe
cure with results fully satisfactory, and can
highly recommend its use. Are not state-
ments of this character, voluntarily made by
reputable business men, worthy of confidence
If not, why not ?
Many people seeing the words “Microbe
Killer” have been led to believe that it is a
disinfectant. It is not a disinfectant, but a
powerful medicine, for while it kills the germs of
disease, it does it in a different manner. It is
taken internally or applied externally as the case
may require. It attacks and kills the microbes in
the system which are the direct cause of disease.
After these germs are destroyed the medicine will
thoroughly cleanse the blood and tone up the
whole system—in short, it restores to a healthy
condition all the members of the body. Although
this medicine has only been for sale in Australia
a short time the constantly increasing sales bear
witness to the-good it is accomplishing. People
who have been using the Microbe Killer for con-
sumption, kidney and liver troubles, indigestion,
and, in fact, for almost every disease, are loud in
their praises of its virtue. Being composed of
pure water charged with powerful germ destroy-
ing gases it is as pleasant to drink as pure spring
water. Send for the book, “History of Microbes
and the Microbe Killer,” posted free. The head
office is 40 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, but
agencies have been established at 319 George-
street, Sydney ; 17 Hunter-street, Newcastle;
and 2(5 Queen-street, Brisbane, where the medicine
can be obtained.*

rjio THE ELECTORS OF EAST SYDNEY.
Gentlemen, —
Impelled by the strongest feelings of a desire to
render some service to the country at the present
time, I offer myself as a CANDIDATE for the
vacancy caused by the lamented death of the late
John R. Street. Esq.
At this election the premier constituency of the
colony should speak with no uncertain sound.
Every question hitherto before the public sinks
into insignificance when the liberties of the people
are threatened by the audacious proposal now
being promulgated for the federation of the
colonies. I say “ audacious ” advisedly, because
there is absolute certainty that the freedom and
independence we now so happily possess will be
imperilled, and that instead of being ruled as we
were, from D iwning-street before we obtained
responsible Government, we shall be ruled and
governed from the Parliamentary-buildings in
Spring-street, Melbourne, or from some other
junior colony.
I rest my candidature as a protest against
parting with the blessings of liberty we now have.
1 f the Federation as proposed becomes law, the
seat of our government will go, and our grand old
city of Sydney will be ruthlessly deprived of its
well-earned prestige and of much of its trade. In my
judgment, also, the value of property will be
much depreciated, and the general interests of the
great b dy of the people of the colony will be
ser.ously damaged.
I trust that you will sink every other question
at the present time, and by your emphatic vote in
my favour show that this wild and profligate
scheme is disapproved by you.
As a large property owner, and having large
commercial interests throughout the colony, and
in consideration of my far wider range of sen-
timent springing from my long years’ resi-
dence and labour amongst the people of the colony
generally, my candidature I trust will not be un-
acceptable to the large majority of my fellow-
citizens.
Yours faithfully,
WALTER BRADLEY.
Sunnyside, Rand wick,
April 2, 1891.
