816 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, April 8,1891.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
Lady Jersey is an unaffected woman, almost too devoid
of airs for a community which isn’t given to adoring those
in high and purely ornamental places-instance the
manner in which her ladyship addresses her husband by
his Christian name before any crowd. At a big public
show the other day she brought the Earl to “ attention”
by simply (and rather sharply, too) saying “George!”
and the way His Ex. jumped showed who really promised
to love, honour, and obey. Owing to this public fami-
liarity on the part of “M. E. Jersey,” LordJ. is becoming
familiarly known as “George”; and another of its bane-
ful effects is that a well-known magnate who is addicted
to loudly calling his wife by her front name in public is
now encouraged to do it more frequently than ever.
It is as I feared—that portion of society which imitates
vice-regality from the shoe-latchet to the top curl, has
taken violently to what maybe called the Lady Jersey
twitch. It consists of a—er—sniffing movement of the
nostrils accompanied by a slight wag of the head, and
now Sydney society has got hold of it and the late
fashionable stony composure has been shattered into
fragments. As people are thus sheepishly imitative of a
vice-regal peculiarity why don’t they copy it still further
and give their faces a rest from powder and pigments?
Since no longer we have an unimpeachable precedent for
floured noses and ripe-radish cheeks it behoves society
to wash its face and make holocaust of its pearl cream if
it doesn’t want to be mistaken for Tottie.
Lord and Lady Carington presented a pair of diamond
bracelets to Miss Horsfall on the occasion of her weddin"
with Brother Hu pert, and Lady C. also gave the bride her
bay hunter and brown pony. Mrs. “ Ru.” will not, after
all, be separated from her people by the marriage as the
Hon. Rupert No. 1, intends to take up wool-raising in
Riverina where their connubial tent is to be pitched.
The Union Club bachelors led off the race-week ball
festivities with their dance on 31st ultimo at Need’s
rooms, which were thrillingly decorated for the occasion
with pampas, fairy-lights, silk, palms, etc., the dressing,
although the evening was wet, being good. No particular
colour had precedence, long trains were not so much
worn by dancers as they were last year, bouquets (some
very beautiful) were generally carried, and flowers were
worn in the hair. Lord and Lady Jersey were present.
The first Albu concert at Sydney Y.M.0.A., on 31st ult.,
was sparsely attended, although, as the ladies are both
powerful sopranos, they are sure to attract larger audi-
ences when their fame spreads abroad. There is nothin"
the Sydney “ musical ” groundlings like better than to be
pierced and riddled with song as it were, and both Miss
Julie and Miss Rose (pretty women of the Hebrew tvpe)
vocalise in a strenuous way that makes the windows rattle.
It may be said that their pronunciation is good, and that
they attack the sound correctly, and, perhaps, if one
heard them in the Town Hall in soli of the a""ressive
sort, where tenderness of tone was not required, one might
be able to say something very nice; but when a songster,
with a vocal organ as strong as an average chorus, attends
more to force than fervour, people whose “ Crown of
sorrow is rememb’ring happier things ” feel inclined to
wad their ears.
But when all is said, how very few women there are
who sing other than mechanically, with a strong inclina-
to yell? The average woman grinds out pages of
eroticism with no more heart in her voice than "if she
were a tin goddess vocalising by steam, and she is always
more addicted to roll ng her eyes than exercising her
soul. The obvious reason is the feminine lack of, or fear of
originality. Women study a song now as to how it affects
their tutor, and the result is parrotish, even though everv
dot, pause and modulation is faithfully put in.
Thursday at Randwick was off-day in every sense'of the
word, for the rain blew all over the stand and the lawn
hissed and gurgled beneath the feet of fashion, which
couldn’t help revealing as it took a hurried promenade to
keep its nose from turning “green,” that it wore its
silken underskirts tucked. But even on that clammy day
there was a ten-thousand attendance, and the vice-re"al
party were there—not exactly in all their glory, but"as
near to it as circumstances would permit. Lady Jersey
wore a summerish costume in cream trimmed with gold
and silver embroidery under a grey mackintosh, sur-
mounted by a lion net in silver straw trimmed with white
chiffon and pink roses, white fall with a black spot; the
little Jerseys in white and fawn frocks with fawn straw
hats trimmed with fawn ribbon, check mackintoshes with
silk-lined hoods. But the interest centred in Mrs.
Rupert Carington, who occupied a seat in the vice-regal
box until brother Rupert (who looks positively beatific
111 his new character as Benedict) took her away to intro-
duce her to the Members’ Stand. Mrs. “Ru ” i s a slim,
medium-sized lady with very dark hair, a rather
petite face, and small, bright blue eyes. She wore a well-
fitting black gown, the bodice and sleeves trimmed with
the new jewel trimming in “sapphires” set in “gold,”
necksoarf of blue chiffon, black feathered hat with
touches of blue. There was very little dressing. Lady
Innes looked cold in green and cream silk with buttercups
m her bonnet; Lady Constance Combe appeared in an
unseasonable thin pink silk, with black hat; a pretty
society girl had got up a rather striking s watch toilet iii
brown and yellow which suited her perfectly; and a gold-
washed matron in brown had painted herself like a clown
-whitewashed nose and chin, cracking carmine lips, pen-
tailed brows and flaming cheeks.
The usual throng of women, most of them having en-
cored their frocking, made Randwick radiant on the last
day of the autumn meeting, which possesses a kind of
awful attraction for the young man with the sloping fore-
head who is always morally certain that he’s going to
“ pick-it-all-up-yer-know,” and is generally seen negotiat.
ing the home beat on his large tan feet. Sassiety turned
out in battalions, though vice-royalty was not present;
but the dressing pointed the moral that Lady Carington’s
fatal habit of wearing only one frock until creation
groaned has entered into the soul of local highflyers, and
most of the furbelows were old friends. Lady Constance
Combe wore pinkish tweed ; Mrs. Burdekin, in her recep-
tion twilight, with the addition of a lavender gamp ; Mrs.
Rupert Clarke, in a cream silk gown embroidered at the
edge of the flounces and across the bodice with bunches
of violets and leaves, leghorn hat and feathers, green
chiffon gamp ; Mrs. (Dr.) Bowker, in dark-blue with a
mottled pattern in brown circles, bonnet with lavender
butterfles ; Miss Bowker, in green with scarf of maize and
blue chiffon, black hat; Miss Nellie Hill, in blue and
green tartan made with a vest of green silk, black Tor-
eador hat, with pompoms and tartan gamp en suite ; Miss
Ethel Lamb, in white with blue chiffon scarf, black hat
trimmed with kingfishers ; Miss Fosbury, amethyst cloth
and velvet, black hat with touches of amethyst; Miss
Marks, in royal blue cloth braided in black with black
girdle, black hat trimmed with grey birds; Mrs. W.
Carson, in fawn and red flecked cheviot, vest in red,
black and fawn, fawn felt hat trimmed with birds; Miss
Maud Galbraith (with Mrs. E. Terry’s party), in moonshine
green trimmed with emerald velvet, hat to match ; Carrie
■Swain, dark purple velvet, flat grey felt with grey and
purple feathers, etc. Quite a feature of the afternoon
was the music, which, though not high class, was very
sweet, a selection from “Gondoliers” being rendered
most soothingly.
Now, as to what will be autumnally the “go” in Sydney
(always shown at the Sydney autumn meeting) it is
certain that straight plain skirts finished with a deep hem
stitched in silk, with Newmarket coats with pockets and
buttoning over frontally to reveal sometimes a dicky and
necktie, and small Toreador hats trimmed with pompoms
to match the costume, will reign all winter. Such an
ugly wooden, relentless sort of style was never before
seen outside Noah’s Ark or stained glass, and the woman
with wide feet who comes forth in the new board-like
skirt with no final frill to break the line of her imposing
pedestals, will be severely criticised as she goes over the
mud.
1 am told on the very worst authority that a Sydney
Government Department intends shortly to engage a
whole horde of lady “ typewritists ” who are going to be
a “great economy to the country”—of course. It is
woman’s unalterable fate, apparently, to undersell her
brother in the labour-market, and there seems to be no
cure for it. Your Sappho is no petticoat politician, still
it is plain enough even to me that if women demanded
men’s wages they might do so until everything turned
green, since men would get the work and the malcontents
might go and hang their typewriters on the willow, in
this particular instance.
There is another side to the Celestial cook-question. A
an up-country surveyor’s tent the other day the “boss’’
sent John, the Chinee cook, packing, calling him sundry
uncomplimentary epithets which at last roused him to
retaliate, “Who are you calling dirty debbil ? Me! no
feah, you dirty debbil! Y'ou eatee anythiny." Which,
considering how graphically The Bulletin has shown how
Chinese cooks make stuffing, was raFner a well-merited
facer, don’t you think ?
The Jerseys’ maiden ball on 2nd inst. was attended by
all the COO invited guests, though the evening was dread-
fully wet at starting-hour—half-past nine o’clock. The
ball was in one sense not a great success, as too many
invitations had been issued, and the crush was dreadfuh
besides which lots of “ lovely waltzes” had to be sat out,
willy-nilly, on the piazza, which was cosily arranged with
many flirtation nooks. The ball was opened with the
usual square, Lord Jersey dancing with Mrs. P. O. Fysh,
Lady Jersey with Treasurer M'Millan (report hath it that
he is engaged to be re-married), Sir George Innes with
Lady Jersey’s eldest daughter, and Mr. Fysh with Lady
Innes. Some of the dressing was good. Lady Jersey was
in wine-red brocade mingled with ivory satin and gold and
silver passementerie, diamond ornaments and bouquet of
cream and crimson roses and ferns tied with the two colours
—the two little Jerseys (some people with fixed ideas on
the subject of the age of debutantes were rather sur-
prised to see them in the festive swim) in cream surah
frocks with lace insertion and long sashes; Mrs. Rupert
Carington and Mrs. Billyard Leake wore their bridal
array; Mrs. Forbes Angus appeared in sunset silk and
roses; Mrs. Burdekin wore a trained gown of cream
satin brocade, &c., &0., &o. ; most of the striking gowns
having done duty at the Bachelors' ball. The next dance
is to happen on 10th inst.
Mayoress Burdekin’s farewell public reception in the
Centennial Hall was singularly free from the lady in red
satin and black curls who brings her daughter in blue and
yellow, and her niece in pea-green. But the frocking
was nearly all resurrected racing-gown. Mrs. Burdekin
wore a white grass-cloth embossed in white silk flowers
and trimmed with silver passementerie, flat hat trimmed
with mauve chiffon, and carried a magnificent presenta-
tion-bouquet of white flowers and maidenhair; Miss
Burdekin appeared in grey figured pongee and gold and
silver insertion, white hat with butterfly-bow of white
chiffon ; Lady Innes in smoke-grey and primrose, the
neck finished with gold braid, primrose chiffon parasol
and buttercups in hat; Mrs. A. J. Riley in black and
vieux-rose; Miss Blomfleld in green silk and gold
passementerie ; Mrs. Haviland Reynolds in black jetted
silk, bonnet en suite ; Miss Frances Saville in heliotrope
brocade made with a deml-train, and trimmed with
puffings of heliotrope chiffon, bonnet in black and lilac
blossoms; Miss 51. Windeyer in rock-melon-yellow with
embroidery yoke, white hat and feathers; Mrs. Robert
Guy in heliotrope silk—and so on, ad infinitum. The
usual array of cakes and ale was furnished forth in the
cold and silent tomb-I mean the basement—which gave
quite a ghoulish air to the feeding—indeed, one woman
with long, grey fangs, who was gnawing her way through
a large, yellow apple, looked, in the damp gloom of the
place, so like an afreet making a meal of a human skull
that your Sappho was glad to hurry upstairs again where
a band was pelting dance-music at the crowd from the
gallery.
Miss Jessie Ackerman, the vigorous temperance advo-
cate, who keeps the road clear for her cause by hitting
adverse critics with her umbrella, gave a well-padded
lecture, on “The Women of Asia,” on 2nd inst., assisted
by well-known Sydney ladies, en costume. The lecture
was not all original, being mostly cut-and-dried informa'
tion to be found in any book of Eastern travel, but it seemed
to suit the audience, which was headed by Bishop and
Miss Saumaurez Smith, and the Sydney ladies were
all there to be looked at. One temperance Yum Yum
(though she hadn’t the Nellie Stewart toddle) looked very
Japanesy, paint and all, in grey silk gown beautifully
embroidered but fortunately without three pins in her
hair—which would never have done in a Christian
gathering. And then we had a model of a Chinese bride, a
silent figure robed in red from head to heel, topped by a
flat, red hat, the face entirely concealed by a fringe,
which drew forth pitying remarks all round ; but I
couldn’t help thinking how much more modest she looked
than our own decollette sacrifices on the altar of Hymen.
There is no dressing in the “ Schoolmistress ” at Sydney
Criterion—excepting Maggie Moore’s final splendour of
theatrical mauve and white and gold—and whatever does
Pattie Brown (who has recommenced rolling her eyes so
violently that one wouldn’t be surprised to see the con-
ductor handing them back to her on a fork) mean by
appearing as a governess in the first act in a seedy brown
wrapper with the flounce pinned up here and there and a
dingy handkerchief hanging out of her pocket? It quite
destroys the probabilities, since governesses—like bank,
clerks - are obliged to keep neat and smooth and un.
patched on ten shillings a week.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
A Melbourne woman has entered an assault-charge
against her butcher, but all the corroborative evidence she
can procure is that of a neighbour who saw her scudding
down the street throwing mutton chops at the fast-
retreating tradesman.
On the vice-regal visit to Campbelltovvn Encampment
on 31st ult., Lady Jersey wore heliotrope silk figured in
white and trimmed with darker velvet, bonnet en suite;
tbe little Jerseys in white frocks and blue sashes, white
hats trimmed with chiffon.
Lord and Lady Jersey visit Maitland (N.S.w.), on 14th
inst.—the day after the vice-regal April dance.
The marriage of Mr. Arthur Allen and Miss Ethel Lamb
will be solemnised in England, whither both culprits
shortly repair. Miss eight’s wedding with Captain
Barlow will take place in Sydney on an early date.
A number of society people are engaged in working up
a monster benefit for Signor and Signora Majeroni, who
have lately met with great misfortunes in the way of
illness. The undertaking is one which will meet with
cordial sympathy from the theatrical profession, the
public and the press.
They say the Ladies Estella and Dorothea Hope, now
departing for England, have milked six cows every morn-
ing before breakfast, at Mount Macedon. And yet we
have let these gals go out of the country !
Some ladies visited the new Melbourne Cathedral the
other day and were fascinated by the gorgeous altar-
cloth. One asks the verger whether it was made in Mel-
bourne. “ Oh, no, ma’am, it is from England, of course.
You could not get anything of that kind here.” After-
wards they found the cloth was made in Adelaide. The
verger was from Hingland, of course.
A lady slipped down at a Victorian railway-station,
struck her head against the train, and was taken up much
discomposed, but the gallant and thoughtful station-
master came running over with a looking-glass and ex-
claimed, “See, madam, you are not disfigured in the
least!” What a lovely story !
There were few spectators at Rupert Carington’s wed-
ding, but all who saw it agree that the bridegroom looked
quite lonely without a straw in his mouth
The “rustling-skirt” is the very latest. The frou-frou
is produced bv tacking a strip of silk on the lower edge of
the underskirt.
Bezique is the great gambling game among English
ladies. “ Rubicon bezique,” played with four packs, is
the most popular and latest development.
A Melbourne “society” organ lias just distinguished
itself by chronicling in detail the marriage of Hon. James
Williamson’s second daughter with Mr. Francis Power, ot
Melbourne Mint, which it says took place on Ist April!
The bridal gown is microscopically described, also the
bridesmaids’ frocks and the bridegroom's gifts of moon-
stone brooches ; finally, we have details of the wedding-
breakfast and of the bride’s tailor-made travelling-gown.
The day after the alleged marriage the bride walked in
Collins street. The paper had anticipated the wedding
by exactly a week, at the end whereof—that is to say on
April Stir—ail that had been mentioned in prophecy by
the Erodzky would no doubt happen.

A NURSE’S REPORT.
Medical men say that a good nurse in a difficult
case is better than medicine, but when we can get
a good nurse and good medicine, the patient
stands a much better chance of recovery. The
few words of advice given below by Nurse Eliza
King, of Great Massingham, Swaffham, are well
worthy the attention of all readers of this paper.
They are as follows : “I have constantly used St.
Jacobs oil in the various situations I have occupied
as nurse, and have invariably found it exQellent
in a'l cases requiring outward ai plications, such
as sprains, bruises, rheumatic affections, neuralgia,
&c. In cases of pleur sy it is an excellent remedy
well rubbed in. I can strongly recommend it,
after several years’ use and experience. It should
be in every household.” Sister Carolina, St.
Andrew’s Hospital, Glewer, Windsor, writes : “I
take this opportunity of stating that I have found
St. Jacobs oil a must efficacious remedy in gout;
also in sprain and bruises. Indeed, we cannot
say too much in its praise, and our doctor is order'
ing it constantly.”
Henry and Ann Bright, hon. superintendents
of the North London Home for Aged Christian
Blind Women, say that the remedy has proved itself
unfailing; that rheumatism and neuralgia have,
in every case, been removed by St. Jacobs oil—
the remedy referred to —and that many old ladies,
some of them 90 years old, instead of tossing about
in agony, now enjoy a good night’s rest through
its influence. Mrs. Bright says she cannot say
enough in its favor.
Six gold medals have been awarded to St.
Jacobs oil for its marvellous power to relieve and
cure pain. It acts like magic. It cures when
everything else has failed. It has cured people
who have been lame and crippled with pain over
twenty years. It cures rheumatism, neuralgia,
faceachc, backache, sprains, and other bodily
pains. It is an external remedy. It penetrates
to the seat of the disease. It is simple, safe, and
sure.
WITHOUT A TRUMPET BLAST.
Simple merit rarely meets with its reward until
too late to be of value. The friendless patriot
who spends his life in the efforts to get members
ot the right politi al persuasion returned to Parlia-
ment has but small joy in the J.P. ship which
comes to him on y after he has sold out of the
butchering business, and left tiie township in which
he could have wilted up his enem.es from the
dizzy elevation of the Bench.
The plain daughter of the family—who has con-
traered a squint from the constant lontemplation
of that awful wart on her nose darns all the
family stockings, replaces the fugitive button,
regulates the supply o: hash, and washes the
annual and ever recurring baby, has s>-en all her
s sters in orange blossom, but missed her own
chances for the want of a trumpet blast. Yet, she
was admittedly the best of the Jot.
The same rule holds good throughout the whole
social fabric, w.th but few exceptions. One of
these, and the most noteworthy, is the Waterbury
Watch. It came and conquered absolutely on its
own merits. The cheap, lacquered, girucrack
Swiss watches have broken many an honest bush-
man’s heart, but were never known to keep time.
Their case is tin, their works are lead, and their
action as erratic as a C.M.G. on a road contract.
They go for either ten or fourteen hours a day,
according to teinperatnre, and scatter misery as
the Maxim gun scatters bullets. They are
shipped to the colour s as ballast; the dealers’
profit is the cause of their construction, and the
ciass stupidity of the purchaser the reason for
their existence. The mendacious.trumpet of the
advertiser bares forth their alleged excellence,
and the simple-minded man with the grass-seed
in his hair becomes a mark for the finger of scorn
and a target for his own contumely.
The Waterbury came like a dream of delight,
and in its own little raid and unobtrusive way
stole into tho hearts of Australians, and made its
name a household word; a synonym for all that

It recently came out in the Commons that
on 12 of the chief English and Welsh lines,
and on the Caledonian and N.B. railway lines,
out of 55,278 railway servants employed just before
the strike, 33,179 worked for 12 hours daily ; that
22,592 drivers and firemen had to work for 15
hours daily in 69,825 instances, and for over lb
hours in 7341 instances. A resolution to the effect
that in the opinion of the Commons these exces-
sive hours were a scandal on justice, was opposed
by Government and beaten by 17 votes. Railway
Commissioner Eddy (N.S.W.) bails from the
Caledonian line.
Rev. Currie, a Melbourne parson, is now
fairly convinced that the earth is the Lord’s, and
syndicates ought to be spelt with an “i ” in the
first syllable. The unhappy shepherd borrowed
two sums of money from the defunct Premier
Building Society, on a vague spiritual understand-
ing with Jimmy Mirams that lie wouldn’t be
called upon to meet any instalments until Provi-
dence had returned him bounteous profit on his
little spec. Mirams, be says, gave him ttiis com-
forting assurance in the first instance, when he
took the shepherd’s borrowed coin for invest-
ment, and also repeated the glad tidings when
another £125 had to be put through, in the shep-
herd’s name, to meet a little bill due to Mr. John
Ebenezer Gourlay. Then Currie retired to tend
bis flock and wait for the blessed harvest-time.
Little by little he lost faith in Mirams, even in
Ebenezer had his trust become unstable, and when
the congregation sang “.All People who on Earth
do Dwell,” the poor man began to wish be hadn’t
bought any earth to dwell on. He doesn’t seem
to know where his particular chunk of this beauti-
ful world is situated, but he is down in a long
unhallowed list for £800 of instalments, which a
fleshly Court has ordered him to keep on paying
up for ever and ever, amen.
London Lord Mayor Savory's now cele-
brated sermon, which we see double-columned in
the Pall Mall with an old discourse of Spurgeon,
is undoubtedly a flagrant crib either from Spurgeon
or from the man from whom Spurgeon stole his
sermon. Apropos, it is related that a divinity-
student at Spurgeon's college was caught_ red-
handed in a discourse almost identical with a
former utterance by Mr. Spurgeon. The unfor-
tunate trespasser was challenged, and, in reply,
stoutly denied the charge ; chapter and verse were
then unearthed, yet still without eliciting a con-
fession. His fellow-students considered the case
sufficiently grave for a higher court, so the poor
fellow was “carpeted” before the “governor,” a
name of endearment enjoyed by Mr. Spurgeon.
Even then the student denied the insinuation, but
eventually owned to the soft impeachment of
having cribbed from an ancient divine. The boot
was then the other foot. The “governor ” himself
stood arraigned for the heinous crime of
plagiarism, and the matter ended amicably.
Fortune for a few pounds.—Buy land in the
Capital City of West Australia. W. J. Douglas,
96 Bathurst-street, Sydney.

Joe Capp will figure no more amongst the
drivelling funniments of the London Sporting
Times. “The Pote,” “Jope,” “The Coroner,”
and Joe Capp—all those over-rated soakers have
soaked themselves away, and the twaddle they
used to talk to “ Master ” is now talked by some-
body else. “Master” will have to disappear one
of these days, and then the Pink ’Un, denuded of
its last stock joke, will slowly peter out.
At Bethnal-green, London, an inquest was
held, the other day, on an infant that had died at
the ago of one year, and a cold shudder went
through the assemblage when the mother arrived
and proved to be a'girl of 13. Yet, in some of the
manufacturing districts of the northern and mid-
land counties mothers of that age are tolerably
common. A very ordinary expedient in those
parts is to baptise two or more children in one
family by the same name, and then if the eldest
dies the next in order can be sent to work in the
factories at the age of 10 or 11, under cover of a
birth-certificate which gives her age as 13 or more.
Consequently, when the pious world is shocked to
hear of some precocious female who has committed
infanticide, or been jumped on with her husband’s
boots, at the age of 14, it is an even chance that
after all she is only 12 or thereabouts. The girl
of the city slums is a prematurely aged creature,
with an old, weary eye and an expression like a
tired Methusaleh ; and apart from the evidence of
her birth-certificate she might be a thing of yester-
day, or she might have been left over from the
Deluge.
Up to date, the British cable-fiend has
announced on no less than eighteen separate
occasions that “ the Argentine Government is
making strenuous efforts to put its finances on a
more satisfactory footing.” We would like this
monster to understand, once and for all, that Aus-
tralia does not care to the extent of one cockroach
what footing the Argentine finances are on. It
doesn’t care whether they are on one foot or
two, or on their last legs, or whether they are on
any footing whatever. If the Argentine Republic
has no finances at all it doesn’t matter, and if
there isn’t any Argentine this country
will get on just the same. The British speculator
is worried about Argentina because that country
has got a lot of his money, and he suffers from
an erroneous impression that Australia takes a
thrilling interest in his money, and can’t sleep till
he gets it back. On this point he is an ass. The
Australians appreciate a good, healthy revolution,
especially when a king has to take up his port-
manteau and quit; but when anyone keeps tele-
graphing about some miserable bad debts, this
nation rises to explain that it doesn’t care one
solitary howl even if the Government at Buenos
Ayres never pays a cent. The next daily paper
man who publishes the statement that Argentina
is making strenuous efforts to put its finances on
a more satisfactory footing, will perish miserably
in the midst of his sins, for the country is becoming
aggravated.

At Moss Vale School of Arts the other day
someone named Bugbear unsuccessfully tried to
have The Bulletin placed on the index expur-
gatorius. Bugbear denounced The Bulletin as
“a paper which went as near as it could to being
immoral.” Bugbear goes as near as he can to
being a liar.
Germany has refused to allow women to
enter the learned professions. Germany is now
the most powerful and, all round, the most
scientific nation in the world. The strongest
nation will always be the one in which the woman
rocks her own cradles instead of turning them
over to a hired woman who gives soothing-syrup
to the baby so that she, the slavey, may have time
to hug the butcher at the back-gate.
Dear Bulletin, —It is better to be a live
dog than a dead lion. Only three years ago an
old friend pointed out to me the evils of drink.
He had abstained for eight years and saved £500.
Last Christmas he owned freehold property to the
amount of £2000. Now he occupies 8ft. by 4ft.,
and was intestate, and knew of no nearer relative
than Japhet I am alive. Fill ’em up again,
Mrs. Forty-rod. N.B.—I have been engaged
writing temperance tracts, and don’t quite see
how to work this into one.— Hiram A biff.

“Deodorising and Disinfecting Company ” shows
a profit and loss debit balance of £600. The
Queen City of the Sowth seems to like t’owld
smell best.
THE LOSS OF THE SIX HUNDRED.
“Sweetness and Light ” Brigade,
Heed not the loss you’ve made,
Work, and you will bo paid
Back the six hundred ;
Yours not to ask us why
Polks, who prefer to die,
Don’t disinfectants buy—
Write off six hundred.
Odours to right of them,
Odours to left of them,
Odours in front of them
(Whereat we’ve wondered),
Suit Melbourne beau and belie,
Bred in the beastly smell.
Cheaply you charged and—well,
Dropped your six hundred.
At the prevailing mess—
Dirt, filth, and rottenness — :
Long has the heavy Press
Jollied and thundered ;
Meantime the Council winks,
Eats like a hog and drinks
“Long life to all the stinks !”
(We’ve smelt six hundred).
Sweetness and Light Brigade,
Yours is a wholesome trade ;
With cats and dogs decayed
Back streets are lumbered.
Germs fill each open drain,
Men, women, children slain
Weep in their graves, in va'n,
Eor that six hundred.
Though poison fills the air,
Keep heart and don’t despair—
Soon will some bloated mayor
Know he has blundered,
And whisper, ere he dies,
“ Dear pals, deodorise
Then, as your takings rise,
You’ll make six hundred.

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Inique Weekly— Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newapaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
Undue Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brainsi
and has become a vast property, because it pos.
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
.The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of everv Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Kanges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

BRUCE SMITH.
Swimming a Fine Art.
A HEROIC EXAMPLE.
Of late there have been many instances wherein
a knowledge of the art of swimming has proved
of more than ordinary advantage to the fortunate
possessor. Oases, indeed, are daily occurring
wherein it has been manifest that but for such a
knowledge great loss of lue must have occurred,
while, unfortunately, as if to turther prove the
assertion, very many lives have been lost through
neglect in youth on the part of the unfon unates
to acquire the natatory art. Fortunately, the
youth of these colonies take to the water almost
from the time they can toddle, and there is but a
comparatively small minority of each sex amongst
the natives who cannot swim. One of the most
notable examples of modern times of the value of
this accomplishment was afforded in the case of
the ill-fated steamship Quetta, the circumstances
of whose wreck are fresh in the minds of many.
The matter at the time was, indeed, much com-
mented upon, and reference to the subject may be
pardoned at this period. Amongst the passengers
was a young girl, Miss May Lacy, the eldest
daughter of Mr. Tyson Lacy, of St. Helen’s
station, Mackay, who, in company with an uncle
and younger sister, were about to pay a visit to
the old country, and whose portrait appears in
this issue. As may be remembered, she was
amongst the survivors, thanks to the facility with
which she was able to keep afloat. But she
went through a remarkable experience, having
been no less than thirty-six hours in the water
without artificial support. The story of her adven-
tures may perhaps be best told in her own words.
After the melancholy event, and when safe again,
though still suffering from the effects of exposure,
she narrated her experiences to a clergyman on
board thus “ Shortly after nine p.m. I was
sitting in the saloon writing a letter to my mother
when suddenly a very remarkable noise was heard.
I at once rushed on deck and saw all the pas-
sengers in a great state of commotion. I rushed
downstairs again and dragged my sister out
of bed. She came on deck in a nightdress. I
then went to my uncle and said, * Is there any
danger V He said :‘ A little, but you both stick
by me.’ We then went
aft. It was like going
up hill, the ship " as
so high above the
water where we were
going. . • Miss
Nicklin was so kind
and offe r e d to go
do vn and get some
shawls to wrap my
sister in. When we
got aft the ship
suddenly went down.
I found mvself going
down, and when 1
was drinking in the
salt water I thought
I was going to be
drowned. But I came
up again, surrounded
by-Cingalese and
sheep. I felt myself
being pressed down
by them. It was
tar i le. Then I saw
a raft a short distance
out and swam to it
and was dragged on
to it by the purser,
who was very kind
to me. We were
attached to a bigger
raft crowded with
Cingalese. We got
away some distance,
as the Cingalese be-
came very noisy, and
we cut our raft adrift.
I remained on her
with the purser till
we were, as I thought,
two miles from shore,
and as he told me he
could not swim, I
left him and swam
for the shore. I did
not reach it, how-
ever. It was so far
away. I went on
swimming towards
the land, and saw
another raft, on
which were two
Cingalese, and to which I made my way and
got on it. But as they were very excited,
and I thought they might be drunk, I left it and
took to swimming again. It seemed so long. I
don’t remember anything till I saw the steamer
Albatross, which I took to be the Quetta. _ When
the boat came along I didn’t want to get into it,
as I thought it was going to take me to your
ship. I thought you were the steward when you
waved your hand to me on board when I was in
the water, but I knew you as well as Dr. Salter
when I came on board. I thought I had been
staying in a large hotel under the water. I think
I must have been unconscious from time to time.”
Altogether, Miss Laoy was in the water for a
period of over 30 hours, and no one but a most
proficient swimmer could possibly have had the
nerve, in such a trying time to husband her
strength by floating on her back, by which means
she kept on the surface. As she was quite naked
at the time she was rescued, the poor girl was
suffering terribly from the heat of the sun and
exposure, but, though in a precarious condition
for a time, she eventually recovered, and was
able to return to her distracted parents. The
moral to be drawn from this remarkable
story is obviously that it is the duty of every
person—both young and old, and of either sex —to
learn to swim at the earliest possible moment.
How many precious lives, indeed, would have been
preserved if swimming was but a universal ac-
complishment? And in this connection it may
be pertinent to ask whether it should not be made
compulsory in our schools, in the city at any rate,
that children should be taught the art? Baths
could be erected in connection with the school-
buildings at no very great cost, and probably
much more benefit would be derived from instruc-
tion therein than from much of the cramming
which is now the rule. However, in the mean-
time it behoves parents to compel their children,
where possible, to attend the public baths, and to
attend themselves also if they are unab’e to
swim. Amongst the many available spots in
Sydney none are more suitable or convenient than
the Sydney Bathing Companv at their * amons
Natat’orinm in Pitt-atreet. We have previously
referred in terms of commendation to this estab-
lishment, at which the accommodation is of a
very high order. The two large basins, which
are so constructed that no person could drown in
them, are fitted in a perfect manner, and from
their central position they are more easily reached
than any other baths, while the attention paid to
visitors is the subject of general remark. Tfye
hbllrs during which the baths aVe ripened to
the public are so arranged that an oppor-
tunity is afforded to both sexes to take ad-
vantage of the pleasures of sea bathing, while
at the weekly reunions of the dual club on
Wednesday evenings, both sexes can partake of
the healthy exercise together, under certain
restrictions as to membership, &c. Although the
extreme heat of the summer has_ now passed, the
baths are still as largely patronised as ever, due
mainly to the fact that the management have
made arrangements by which the temperature of
the water is raised to 72 deg. Bathing is there-
fore, made a luxury throughout the year at these
baths, and as pipes have been laid by means
of which, when the really cold weather comes on,
the temperature can be raised to 85 deg., there
will probably be even an increased attendance
during the winter. We would recommend all who
have not paid the Natatorium a visit to do so at
the earliest possible moment.
A Rising Locality.
THE POOR MAN’S OPPORTUNITY.
During the last five years the area of the metro-
polis of Sydney has extended to such an alarming
extent that one is constrained to pause sometimes
when passing through the suburbs and ask,
“ Where will it end ?” Go in what direction you
will miles away from the city—and there are
still streets and terraces of houses, all being
erected for the convenience of those now resident
and those still to come. On the western-suburb
line, the suburbs now extend almost continuously
to Parramatta, while south and east and north
houses meet the eye at every turn. It is an evi-
dence of population, of course, for houses will
not be erected unless they are likely to be
tenanted, and they cannot be erected without
money. Therefore, it is to be reasonably assumed
that the stream of population and wealth to the
city is on the increase. Each of these new build-
ings naturally adds to the value of the land in
the vicinity, and so it happens that what five
years ago was perhaps bush or sand, worth com-
paratively nothing, is now sold by the foot, and
yields to its fortunate possessors a wonderful
return on their outlay. In fact, it is beyond dis-
MISS MAY LACY
pute that many large fortunes have been made in
Svdney of late years by speculators who
have, with an eye to the future, purchased
when land was almost being given away, and
who have received the advantage of the
unearned increment of value. _ And, whatever
op nions we may hold upon this question of the
land, there can be no doubt we muT do in Rome
as the Romans do, and where possible in our own
way make money if we can. It is the spirit of
the times, and we must be in it or be left. Per-
haps no more favourable opportunity is presented
for speculation of this character at the present
time than on the north side of the harbour.
Already the population there is increasing very
rapidly, and it is spreading towards the new rail-
way line, in which direction it may fairly be
assumed the city will extend in the future. Indi-
cations are rife that a great part of the shipping
business of the port will gradually centre about the
North Shore, and when the railway is complete to
the shores of Port Jackson, a wonderful fillip
will be given to land values along the line.
Shrewd, hard-headed men, who observe this fact,
are purchasing along the line with a view to the
rise in values, and no spot is receiving greater
attention in this direction than Hornsby and its
vicinity. Much - peculation is going on there and
but little available land remains for the outside
public. An opportunity will, however, be afforded
on Saturday to those who are desirous of securing
the site for a residence in one of the most charm-
ing localities out of Sydney, or who wish
to buy with a view to future possibilities.
On that occasion Messrs. Thorpe and Co.,
of Parramatta, will sell on behalf of the
British Australian Land and Banking Co.
(of 161 Hoffnung’s Buildings, Sydney) a fine
property known as the Mount J ersey Estate,
which lies adjacent to the railway line, between
Hornsby and the Colah platform. This land is
splendidly situated on very high ground, being
the most elevated point in Hornsby, and when
the North Shore line is completed it will be within
the same distance of Sydney as Parramatta is in
another direction. The views to be obtained in
the vicinity are superb, while the soil has been
proved to he very fertile by the numerous
orchards which abound. In fact, no finer
orange trees or passion vines are to be seen
in the colony than at Hornsby, and as this will be
the junction of two lines—the North Shore and the
Great Northern- the district will be well served
by rail. In fact, there is every indication that in
the not very distant future Hornsby will be
settled with a larg'e and well-to-do population
when land obtained at the present time will in*
crease immensely in value. The Company
certainly offer their clients every inducement to
purchase. The deposit on one lot will be £B, in-
creasing by £1 per lot on each lot taken by one
bidder ; while instalments of 2s. a week only will
be required upon each lot. A considerable area
can thus be secured upon remarkably easy terms.
A special train will run to the Instate from -hed-
fern station on Saturday afternoon, for which
passes can be obtained on application, and there
should be extensive competition at this sale.

SUICIDE OR NO SUICIDE.
The New York Life Insurance Company-
Pays Its Policy.
It being evident that most cases of self-destruc-
tion involve causes not under the victim s own
control, and that accident and murder may easily
o-ive rise to a suspicion of voluntary death, the
ss r ew York Life Insurance Company, in order to
prevent wrongs to the unfortunate, makes its
policies absolute security. It exercises caution in
granting a policy, and never issues one unless its
officers consider the applicant a good risk. But
when it has once issued its policy and taken the
premium, it feels bound to insure. It does not
insert in its policies the usual clause making them
void in case of “self-destruction, voluntarily or
involuntarily, sane or insane,'’ and claims no re-
lease from its contract except where there is
evidence of a purpose to defraud. In short, so
far as a life policy may properly be such, the hew
York Life’s policies are incontestable from the
moment the first premium is paid. The man with a
New York Life Insurance policy may feel sure that,
in case of his death, no delay, litigation or loss will
arise from the question of self-destruction
whether it be accidental or otherwise. If he has
been honestly insured, and honestly conforms to
the simple requirements of the contract, the pay-
ment of his policy is sure on proof of his death.
Chief offices, department of Australasia, corner ot
George and Bond streets, Sydney.

Tales of the Old Regime.
No. IV.—Convict Smith the Second.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When Dr. Walsh, 'Catholic Bishop of
Nottingham, conveyed his episcopal congratula-
tions and blessings to that devoted priest, Robert
William Willson, on his consecration to the See of
Hobart Town, V.D.L., he remarked: “It seems
to me that I could make myself as happy in
accompanying you to Van Dieman’s Land as re-
maining in England. 1 would cheerfully obey an
order from Rome to that effect. The way to
Heaven is quite as short from Hobart Toivn as from
Nottingham. ”
The truth of the italicised passage is as it may
be, but Convict James Smith, per George 111.
(Second) was distinctly of opinion that the path
to hell from Hobart Town was much shorter than
from Nottingham. In Nottingham he was only
sentenced to be hanged. At Hobart Town they
the Law, Justice and the Authorities (who
occasionally took it into their heads to act with
the former parties) not merely threatened to dis-
locate Mr. Smith’s vertebras, but actually did so.
Now Convict Smith the Second’s notion of
distances was, let us tell you, not by any means to
be despised. .For according to all accounts (which
included his penal record) he was tolerably well-up
in that branch of the science of surveying, known
as road -ad m eas urem en t.
* # #
Had, however, he not been quite so pro-
ficient, the chances are that he would have re-
mained engaged in surveying roads in Notts
country instead of helping, for a short time, to
make them with pick, shovel and crow-bar (as a
preliminary to being hanged) in the neighbour-
hood of Glenorchy, suburb of Hobart Town in
Van Deinonia. _ Sometimes it happens that a
man’s strength is his weakness. This was the
explanation of Convict Smith the Second's trip to
the Southern World. He trusted too implicitly
to his capacity for estimating distances.
* # #
In the year ’B3, the County Surveyor
of Nottingham, ( one of whose apprentices
was Mr. James Smith, afterward James Smith
the Second, ex ship George HI., received
a commission from a London publisher. The
latter was engaged in bringing out a new edition
of that excellent and popular work, “The
Traveller’s Companion, or a Delineation of the
Turnpike Roads of England and Wales,” first
issued by Mr. John Cary, engraver and map-
printer, of the Strand, forty years before, and the
County Surveyor was required to revise all the
local information as to the main-roads, cross-
roads, and the gentlemen’s seats in Notts country.
The Surveyor being, of course, a big man, and
entitled to move in the best society—had not the
Lord-Lieutenant himself extended him one finger
at a county festival three years ago ?—did not
■ propose to do the work himself. " Not lie ; he
simply told Smith, an adolescent customer who
often got into scrapes just for a love of ingeniously
getting out of them, to spend a month on the job.
“ You pan lend me your gig, sir?” had queried
Apprentice—and soon-to-be transport—Smith.
“ No, indeed !” snapped in reply the big man.
How am I to get about if you’ve the gig and
mare 9 You must do it on Shanks’pony, James,
unless you can borrow a horse. Why, when I
was your age, I’d have walked the country twice
over, with pleasure, for the sake of the outing.”
Mr. Smith, barely out of his teens,
enjoyed an outing, no doubt, when there was a
fishing-line or a pretty girl to share it, but he
scarcely fancied a month’s pedestr an exercise
™ no other company than an old copy of
Cary,” a note-book and a pencil, and as his own
purse would not permit him to hire a horse for the
period of his survey, he made up his mind to beg
or borrow a steed whenever and wherever he
could.
By carrying out this resolve he was successful
in getting over much ground and through part of
his task before the moment came for him to
traverse the cross-road from Nottingham town
towards Matlock. He had ridden along the Great
Northern Road to the borders of Yorkshire, and
had scoured the eastern parts from Cringely-on-
i hll to where the old Roman way strikes at
Bingham the route over a point of Leicestersliire
to Grantham. lie had seen all the great
houses within the country limits and on
the borders from Welbeck to Bel voir Castle,
and, as he was not comple’ely emancipated
from trie By runic stage of manhood, had mooned
unmeloaiously in the precincts of Newstead
Abbey, and had spouted “ Don Juan ” and
Cluldo Harold ” to the wondering and un-
applausivo deer. Altogether ho had enjoyed
himself, for the season was a late-continuing
autumn, and though the warmth of summer days
had long vanished, something of their glow and
beauty still streamed through long aisles of
majestic oaks and into the mossy chapels which
Nature had carved in the trunks of great beeches.
When, .however, he came to describe the vicinity
of the road to Matlock there was a change. He
had so intruded ujion the kindness of such of his
friends as owned hacks that he felt he could not
trespass further upon their courtesy, and a re-
newed application for the loan of master’s gig
met with no more success than his first.
“Why, ’Lis only 14 miles to Somercote’s,” had
retorted, to his request, the County Surveyor.
You con'd get theie and back in a day and
walk it easily.”
‘'Not and do the gentry’s seats. There’s the
great house between Bobber’s Mill and Cinder
Hill, then there's the Sedley’s and Nuthall—the
park and armoury there’ll take a couple of days,
and there’s Mr. Rolleston’s by Watnal and
Greasley .Church. It’s not the straightforward
work I mind, but the going and coming. That’s
what wears me out.”
But the County Surveyor was adamant, and
Mr. James Smith had about concluded that he
would have to complete his work of survey solely
and purely by the aid of his own feet when a
thought struck him. Why should he not requisi-
tion from Gipsy Bill, the Ostler at the Blackmoor
Inn, the loan of one of the numerous riding-hacks
which wer- constantly being baited at that well-
known and highly-esteemed hostelry? Bill and
he were great Cronies—Bill had acted the artless
part to him of guide, philosopher, and friend in
the matter of those springes in Nuthall Stony;
Bill would surely do as much for hi n, especially
if his appeal were backed up by a shilling and a
deep draught of the Blackmoor’s nut-brown ale.
So Bill was applied to, and intimated, like the
then uninvented Barkis, “that he was willin’.”
If Mr. Smith would promise to start before day-
light on the morrow and to be back within 48
hours—well, there was young Mr. Blount’s pet
roadster, three-quarter blooded, in the stable,
and Mr. Blount would not return from the capital
for a couple of days at earliest, and—“p’r’aps
longer, Meester Jem, fur them young sirs hain’t
m none un hurry to a-coom bak w’en them’s
once in Lunnuh town.” Which remark was so
true that the _ soon-to-be-transported Smith
promptly recognised its truth and consummated
the bargain with a pot of beer and the shilling.
Mr. Blount’s three-fourths-blooded gelding
might have been fully-blooded, so rapidly did he
carry young Smith over his ground the next day.
Jim had never crossed his legs on such a bit of
horseflesh before. He knew something about the
noble animal in general, and he realised that it
would be a long day, once lie stabled this noble
animal at the Blackmoor, before he sat astride
another such a splendid creature as it was.
* # *
‘‘ Well, sir,” said Jim, relating his trouble
to Father Nellathorue in Hobart Town * a few
days before In's execution, twelve months later—
Jim was an Anglican, but he would have nothing
to do with Parson Ford—“the Devil was in that
horse. Believe me, sir, I had no thought of
stealing it.”
“ I believe you, Smith.”
1 hank you, sir. But Lord—oh, I beg your
pardon, Father, I wasn t thinking.”
“Nevermind, my man, go on. Keep to your
story - you haven’t much time.”
That s true, sir. But ’twas a horse of grand
fettle. I did the distance to Selstone in no time,
as I could take him ’cross fields and over hedges.
At Selstone, I could have stopped, but I went in
to Somercote’s, and from Somercote’s I must go
in to Matlock, jut for the pleasure of the ride, sir.
Why, I felt as though I’d never lived up to that
moment, Father!”
“ Yes, yes, I can understand. But go on,
Smith—time is so precious, my poor fellow.”
Priest Nellathorue could appreciate the feeling
which, even now, in his dire circumstances,
flushed the convict’s cheeks with the remembrance
of those few hours of genuine delight. Than
Father Nellathorne and Bishop Willson at a later
date, not even old Quorn Hall Clarke himself was
a better judge of, ora more fervent lover of, the
horse. Bishop Willson once saved a convict’s
neck because he could break-in a hitherto un-
tameable colt. There was, by the way, no des-
cription of vicious brute, from a Norfolk Island
Commandant and a Yandemonian Governor-
General to a lunatic and a bushranger, that the
Bishop did not tame.f
Smith continued : “Then the Devil being in
the gelding or in me—”
“In you !” interrupted the priest.
u “ P’ r ’ a ps,” said the ironed convict.
“ Satan’s come to many a man in the shape of a
horse. Then, when I got to Matlock, 2(i miles
from Nottingham—SO, p’r’aps, as I’d come—l
baited him at the Eagle and Child public-house,
and I had a snack myself. Chatting in the stable-
yard, I heard there was a meet of the hounds at
"See The Bulletin, March 21, 1891: “Convict Arden’s
Yellow-heart.”
f It may here be remarked that (he somewhat emphatic
distinction which has been, and maybe, drawn in these
“ Tales ” between the devotion and self-denying energy
displayed by priests and bishops of the Roman Catholic
faith and the qualities manifested by clergymen and
dignitaries of the Church of England, is not due to any
peculiar reverence possessed by the author for “Mother
Church.” In making the distinction, he is simply fol-
lowing the trend of historic fact.
Melton-Mowbray on the morrow, and although
Melton was full 20 miles the other side of Notting-
ham, I made up my mind I’d go there that night,
put up at a farm belonging to an aunt of mine
close by and see the meet next day. Well Jiat I
did. 1 left the Nottingham pike at Radford,
skirted the town so that I should not be seen,
and so reached the farm. I stopped there the
night—my aunt was so pleased to see me that she
never asked what I was doing in the neighbour-
hood or how I’d got there; and the next day I
went to the throw-off of the hounds, the gelding,
sir, being as fresh in the morning as though he
had not done 70 miles the previous day.” Here
the Condemned stopped to gather up his recol-
lections, and to feel once more, the thrill of
physical joy that had gladdened in the course of
that tatal ride.
Ah, sir, though it was my last free day, ’twas
a glorious one! I'm not sure,” and here he
brought his leer-irons together with a clank, “that
I wouldn’t go through what I’ve passed through
since to have such another day.”
“ Hush, my son ! Don't think of that, now.
Think of—of ”
“The hangman, Father! Well, sir, I will.
Oh, and by the way, sir, do you know who’ll do
the scragging on my day '! i hear Johnson’s at
Oatlands.”
“ Smith, I fear you do not yet understand or
realise your awful position. I consented to listen
to your story as j on asked me, but this irrelevancy
of thought is very terrible. Try, my man, and
fix your mind on your doom—and your God.”
And the devoted priest adopted a sternness of
tone which his own heart rebelled against as cruel,
though his reason approved of it as just. It was
intensely afflicting to a mind to whom this world
was the Shadow, the Unseen, the Substance to
witness, as the few such minds embraced in the
colonial population were almost weekly called
upon to witness, the indifference and frivolity of
condemned men* The awful subversion of the
moral-sense which it was the chief aim of the
Regime to effect was a process never completed
till till the creaking of the rope on the beam
told that again the System had once more
usurped the function of the Creator.
* * *
James Smith the Second went on with his
story—first apologising to the priest.
“ Well, I went to the meet. I intended to go
to see the throw-off and then go home. But with
the first cry of the beagles the gelding dashed
away, and over hedges and ditches and through
copse and spinney, and ’cross ploughed field and
fallow, I went with him. ’Twas a magnificent
run a regular rattler, and it lasted for ten miles.
I was not in at the death, no wonder, considering
what the nag had already done. I was, however,
close up, but the gelding lame at the finish.
They had killed near Strawston village. All at
once my situation flashed upon me. The gelding
would hold up, maybe, for another ten or twelve
miles, but I was thirty from home, going back
the w'ay I came. And then I made my mistake.
I got somehow to think the road by Grantham
was only twenty. It was an awful blunder, that.
Had I returned to my aunt’s, and told the story,
it might not have been too late to have got a
messenger to the Blackmoor. But, thinking it
was only twenty miles, I thought I’d chance it
with the horse through Grantham. Walking
and leading I thought to reach the inn before
daylight. But it was twenty-nine miles, and it

had been daylight for three hours before I
reached the inn. Near the Town Hall I was

The transport paused in his story, as though he
expected an exclamation of surprise from the
priest. None came—Father Nellathornc was by
this time too well versed in the history of British
criminal practice to manifest wonderment at so
very probable an occurrence as the arrest of a
man for horse-stealing, just as he was about to
return the beast to its stable. He knew of worse
cases than that.
“Gipsy Bill, it appeared, had heard that I had
been seen on the road by Melton-Mowbray, and,
as I had told him I was going in the different
direction, had become fearful of my intentions,
He told the landlord—of course, putting a good
face on his own share in the transaction—and he,
in his turn, got alarmed at the prospect of olfendl
liig Mr. Blount, a first-class customer, and of
being held responsible for the horse. He waited
till within an hour of the arrival of the London
coach, by which he expected young Mr. Blount
back, and as I had not appeared, knocked up the
Chief Constable and a magistrate, and obtained
a warrant. You know the rest, Father, vdu know
the rest.”

rpi ■ . i--, , ■, v- i
The priest did know it. You, however,
reader, do not, and, therefore you must be told,
Very commonplace, very trifling, no doubt, was
the before-related incident, and yet, after all was
it not very human ? In these narratives of ola
colonial times, there is, we fear, much that is
Commonplace ana vulgar, but we cannot help
that Beneath the coarse garb of the frieze which
elothed he male transport and the checked print
Which clad the female, there, had, once upon a
time, beat human hearts and pulsed human emo-
turns. Once upon a tune !-for the men of the
Regime took good care hearts and emotions were
not tolerated in their System, They could feel
acutely for a prince or a peer who suffered from
toothache, and might, on rare occasions, wince in
sympathy when the hand of Death fell heavily on
a freeman’s home. But a convict’s aspirations ! -
a prisoner s emotions ! Tush ! Nonsense ! No
“ w r' g
, We have ventured to differ from the men of the
Regime, and to follow the lead Of a judge like
1 orbes rather than Stephen, of Anglican clergy-
men like Naylor rather than Marsden, of Catholic
priests hke iherry and Nellathornc, McEnroe
and Willson rather than Governors hke Darling
Arthur and Denison, of officials like Crouch
rather than Price. And, treading thus in the
footsteps of our guides we have come to a point
whence wc can hear, stealing through the corridors
the wail^of 1 crash°oTshattered
lives. Something else, too, we hear-at longer
intervals, but still audible: the echo of a chant
as the angels note some sublime act of renunoia
tion. You will find no such deed recorded in
registers and reports and minutes But a few
such there are all the same ; visible to him who
wiH read in the henna of ghastly ntnnerioal
n.ty becomes mcm-sancl when it scales the mount
of self-sacrifice, then some half-a-dozen convicts’
lives have, m their current or their ending,
redeemed even the System from its in amy
Why, this? Because the “rest,” so briefly
referred to by Convict James Smith the Second,
was a deed of that kind.
* * *
When the transport ship George 111.
obliged the Regime by foundering in D’Eutre-
castreaux Channel, among the convicts who
carried their opposition to Law and Order to such
an extent as to refuse to drown were Convicts
James Smith the Second and James Smith the
Fourth. James Smith (1) and James Smith (3)
were considerate enough to remain in the un-
fathomed depths of Ocean.
When the ship left “home” in addition to an
assortment of other but diversely pre-naraed
Smiths, she had on board four of the family who
answered to “James.” Which, though, by no
means unprecedented, was rather perplexing.
For instance, the First and Fourth James were
“ lifers,” while the Second and Third were only
short-termers-seven and ten years respectively,
“ Lifers ” had certain privileges (such as wearing
irons more frequently) which were not granted to
less fortunate short-term men. It would not have
been half so troublesome a matter had all four
been due for “life,” and, indeed, the Surgeon-
Superintendent seriously took counsel with him-
self as to whether he should not alter the manifest
so as to make their sentences uniform. He would
not, of course, have made ’em all short; he would
have extended ’em all to “life” fame* tbe

PRICE WARUNG.
The Toper's Confessional.
Ix many cookery-books it is taken for granted
that the stomach and brain to be experimented
upon belong to an individual in the most robust
health, who has never heard the chimes at inul-
niglit, been hurled out of a pub., nor ordered
devilled bones, heated to seventy times their
wonted heat, at daybreak. In this article, whilst
the farm labourer, the athlete, the steeplechase-
rider, the student, the burglar, and the teapot-
sucker will be instructed, the'interests of the
toteetaller—(kindly print as spelt)—will not be
neglected.
Happy the man who can respond to the matu-
t nal rat-tat at h s bedroom door with a cherry
“ Come in, William, and you needn’t put any hot
water in my bath this morning.” Thrice happy
he who can raise his head from his pillow of down
without qualms as to it (the head, I mean) rolling
from off his shoulders. Such knights of virtue
and integrity need no restorative nor eve-
opener, but can boldly attack that most
loathsome and terrible of all operations—the
morning’s toilet—with equanimity and without
adventitious ami. kbit some there be, I grieve
to say, who, having mislaid the key of the tantalus
over night, have perforce been compelled to lie
awake, tossing and groaning, enduring all the
tortures of the starving traveller in the arid
desert, until somebody -was stirring wdio could
procure th°m a morning draught.
Said draught takes in my and various forms.
1 ea is one of these, but 1 prefer hot-water, which
latter beverage is said by not a few experts to be
the finest pick-me-up oh ihe inhabited globe,
kluid magnesia is bur seldom used nowadays
with so many patent restoratives in the market!
With a choice between brandy-and-soda and
bitter beer, give me the latter —albeit, a gout pro-
vocative. Our ancestors, who knew a trick or
two in the drinking line, invariably cooled
their coppers on small ale, quaffed out of
richly-chased silver tankards. Some friends of
my youth will smile when they read that
I cannot commend brandy-and-soda as a useful
morning’s draught, though ’tis. doubtless, a pic-
turesque one in the extreme. The great fault of
this species of quencher is that it is so very
more-ish.” You always want another—and you
usually have one. Twopenn’orth more, and up
goes the vital force, only to fall down-derry-down
again within an hour or so. ’r he after-effects of
soda-water, it cannot be too widely known, are
depressing ; and this I say as a poor wretch who
lias strutted and fretted on Life’s stage for nearly
ha'f a century, and who during that tim Q has been
somewhat of a friend to the race of Bchweppe.
Hr and y by itself is better, and more s vtisfying ;
and Worcester sauce, with a dash of cayenne m
it, w,!l revive most corpses ; but the latter is apt
to breed a heart-burn like a blast furnace. Now,
listen, my bonnie boys ; the best awakeper and
general cheerer-np amongst chamber-drinks that
I know is a doctor. I don’t mean a thing of drm»s
and lancets, but the following blessed and soothing
concoction, which, if none of you have ever
sampled for eye-opening purposes, you should try
at once.
Put the yolk of a new-laid egg in a big tumbler.
Cover the mouth thereof with a piece of thick
paper—a writ or summons will do—and shake up,
This is a quicker operation than whacking into the
egg with a fork, and you don’t always break the
tumbler. Stir in a good wineglassful of the oldest
brandy you can get credit for, and then fill up to
the ‘ pretties ” with new milk. Add,a little sifted
sugar, mix well and gulp down. You can go
hunting,- or attend a meeting of creditors, in an
hour’s time, with safety, even if you happened
to retire to rest a few hours before, with your
hat on.
If the stomach is crying out for solid nourish-
ment, an anchovy toast is absolutely necessary.
But how few know the secret of concocting this
gentlemanly dish !
H ere you are.
Have ready a hot water plate, or if you haven’t
one, a slop-basin, filled with boiling water, with
an ordinary plate atop will do. On this melt a
piece of butter, the size of a walnut, and stir into
this the yoke of one egg. Then add two table-
spoonsful of anchovy sauce, add a dash of cayenne,
and stir well. Then get some thin slices of bread
toasted to a delicate brown, and hot. Well soak
in the mixture, and wolf. You will, probably
ask for more.
Never under any circumstances take a cold bath
the morning after a “heavy” night. It checks
the action of the heart, congests the liver,
paralyses the lungs, and will counteract all the
good effect which has been produced bv what you
have swallowed between the sheets. Hot-hot as
you can bear it, my children, if von have a bath
at all. Another way. Don’t have "a bath. What's
the matter with to-morrow morning, anyhow ?
Breakfast is a meal which, if possible, should
be eaten in solitary state. The brains of but
few people are in a condition to eon'rol conversa-
tion before noon. This, of course, does not apply
to milkmen, chimney-sweeps, and other worthies
who amass large fortunes through picking up the
early worm. The necessity for continual passing
to and fro of teacups, coffee-cups, and cocoa-cups,
all loaded to the brim, Occasionally adds a terror
to the breakfast-table, which makes the nervous
man to resolve in future to, break his fast within
the security of his own chamber. What a fearful
ordeal it is, in a large house, where you are a
visitor, to be the last down to breakfast—more
especially should your host err on the side of
punctuality, and dose his household with family
prayers each morning at nine to the tick ! I have
not infrequently gone through this ordeal.
“Good morning, Mr. dobbins,” observes the
lady of the house, with freezing politeness.
Afraid I m a little unpunctual,” I murmur.
“Not a bit of it, my dear boy,” shouts the host,
sarcastically— he always shouts at you, does your
host. “ You’re extremely punctual to your usual
time, half-an-hour after everybody eise.” And
then adds, witli the grin of a fiend -“I hope you’ll
be aide to shoot as straight as you made the spot
stroke last night.”
“ Looks a bit white about the gills, don’t he? ”
chuckles Dick Lawless, who has the digestion of a
plough boy and the nerves of a parachutist.
“ Yes,” replies the prettiest girl in the room
‘ I’m afraid you’re a sad rake, Mr. dubbins !” ’
And then I have to look at the superscription
on my letters, and ponroiß mv own tea-which I
u-'v-r drink, by the way, but furtively emptv into
the slop-basin, occasionally on the‘doth-whim
my host is deep in his paper.
Letters, by the same token, should invariably
be brought up to a man’s bedroom, and not placed
on his breakfast plate. It is always best to know
the worst as speedily as possible ; and you should
consistently hide your woes from your friends who
only laugh at them.
Equally objectionable is a face-to-face breakfast
with the wife of one’s bosom. Hardened repro-
bate that lam, my nerves are not always in the
best of order, as I descend , to the feast of eggs
and bacon and the flow of Qiiong Tart. Still the
issue must be faced with courage. So, with a
cheery word or to, I take my seat, pat the dogs,
who range themselves around my chair, and fall
to, in the hopes that my condition will pass
muster with the inspecting offleer.
Vain hope ! The steel grey eye of the sock-
darner is on me as I raise the cup of Van ITouten
to my lips, and a still stern voice rnurmers :
“ Good gracious, how your hand shakes this
morning ! ”
And I put down my cocoa untasted.
The game is up. I shall have to be in by ten
that evening*, and I might', as well be hanged for a
sheep as a lamb. So, with a sulky nod to the old
Dutch, I adjourn to the library where, over a
small bottle of beer, I grizzle over my woes, and
hunt up the law of divorce.-

The British Workman states that every
time a man swallows a threepenny drink he con-
sumes a square yard of land. This sounds well as
a teetotal argument, but were the land situate on
Circular Quay, feydney, and the Government
going to buy it, one square yard would give 1800
people delirium tremens.

A Lay of the Maori War.
The military hero of the following epic, which
has the merit of being true, is one of the delegates
at the Federal Convention :
The date of this ultra-political lay
Is ’6l, in the month of May,
Or it might have been April, I cannot say
To a year or a month or two or a day;
But ’twas in the time of Governor Grey—
That rare old time, when the Maori bold
Was whipping the cat for the land he’d sold,
A-swearing, ’twixt nips, a most terrible oath,
That the land wasn’t big enough for both
Maori and pakeha—one must go
To somewhere or other, above or below.
He knew the old proverb re quarrels just,
And the virtue of getting his “ blow in fust,”
So he sloshed around in the swampy bush
And bashed in the heads of the pakeha “ push,”
Till at length the white-man’s dander riz,
And, humping his rifle, he went to biz.
Lawyers and cobblers and knights of the shears,
Bank-clerks, doctors and auctioneers
Stepped into the ranks of the volunteers,
And the Regulars furnished the officiers
(Which is bastard French, for the sake of rhyme).
And fine and large they’d a rorty time.
They chivvied the Maoris from Otahuhu
And dispersed a portion to Waiuku,
But a troublesome section settled down
To play high-jinks round Wairoa town,
Or settlement, rather, and looted the farms,
Bagging the settlers’ fowls and arms,
And finally squatting some miles down lower
On the further bank of the river Wairoa.
Bimeby up came the sojers bold,
With their leaders gorgeous in red and gold.
They knew that somewhere among the trees
The niggers were hiding as thick as bees,
So they halted and opened out the prog,
And made a hole in the canteen grog,
While the Colonel chewed the matter o’er
As he smoked and drank and probably swore. -
As night came on he divided iiis force,
Giving commands in a whisper hoarse,
And when the dawn broke far from clear,
He had half his men in the Maoris’ rear;
Thus they were placed, as you will see,
Between Old Nick and the deep, blue sea.
Thus thought at least the Ensign gay
As he gave his orders to fire away,
Knowing the Colonel would do the same,
But the savages dropped to the little game.
Now, the Ensign’s name was Elephant Bill—
He had plenty of figure and lots of will,
But he wasn’t able to tell at all
The rattle of slugs from the whizz of a ball.
And when someone —a corporal—up and said
That the bullets, coming unpleasantly near,
Were undoubtedly regulation lead,
He said with a swear, a snarl, and a sneer,
“ Get back to your place and do as I say,
And blaze at the rascals over the way.”
But the Corporal had come from an editor’s chair,
Was used to being sworn at, and didn’t care ;
So he jumped to his feet and ran down the ranks,
Unheeding the Ensign’s bellowing blanks,
And yelled—“ Cease firing; don’t you see
As plain as ever a thing can be
That the men we are shooting aren’t niggers at all,
But our men ?” And just then the bugle-call
Rang out, and along the further bank
Appeared the soldiers file and rank
Near where the Maoris the night before
Had laid down to rest, but not to snore ;
And Elephant Bill swore a terrible D
And said : ‘ ‘ However can such things be—
To think that a pressman knows more than me
Then he drowned his bad grammar in ration rum
And wished the Maoris in Kingdom Come.
But the newspaper man, whose instinct keen
Had saved the force from what might-have-been,
Was feted and toasted and got his leave,
And went back to his sanctum more lies to weave.
So ends the story of Elephant Bill,
Who erred from want of the proper skill;
Who nearly sent, on that famous day,
A number of souls down the devious way
In the fighting times of Governor Grey.
That wealthy bachelor George Rolfe, the
Melbourne merchant, is about to federate with a
widow. The banns have already been proclaimed.
It will much amuse a lot of people to find
that Dean Mahony (late of Penrith N.S.W.) takes
a prominent part in the Irish movement to down
Parnell for his alleged giddeness.
A Melbourne man charged with stealing a
wheelbarrow pleaded hunger and no work. He
applied, he said, to the Salvation Army for food
but had been refused as he had never been in gaol.
To qualify him for the Army’s Charity the Bench
sent him up for a week to Col. Bull.
We have been reading with pleasure the
autobiography of Rev. J. Bickford, an Australian
parson free from cant, who does not hang upon
the skirts of wealth. The author records that his
gorge rose when in the charging sermon to himself
and other sucking parsons, leaving London for
Sydney, they were implored not to mix in politics;
but Bickford resolved he would, after seeing the
degraded state of the agricultural laborers in
Devon, his native county, wherefore he was known
throughout his Australian career as a staunch
Liberal.
A particularly heart - rending kind of
divorce-suit is disturbing the British aiistocracy.
Lady Russell demands to be put asunder because
her luvin’ ’usband takes revolvers to bed with
him, and when he can’t sleep he fills in the weary
hours by blazing away promiscuously all over the
bedroom. His favourite target, however, is the
footboard of the bed, and his wife is haunted
with a dreadful fear that some night he will shoot
her feet, and then say he did it by mistake. When
she remonstrates, however, he only laughs an
insane, cracked laugh, and begins shooting with
two revolvers at once, and as he is a ghastly shot
there is bound to be a disaster sooner or later.
And, in addition to that, the unfortunate lady is
unable to sleep —who could sleep with a British
peer whooping and doing gun practice at her little
pink extremities, and plunging and gambolling all
over the place with firearms in each hand and a
big, black cigar in his sinful jaw ? and she is
getting haggard and old-looking through anxiety ;
and as she doesn’t want to go home to her mother-
on crutches, or to return on her hands with her
feet coming behind her on wheels, she has resolved
to give the whole thing up.

Messrs. Lichtner and Co., Pitt-street,
Sydney, have at their offices a 3500-year-old sar-
cophagus of an Egyptian mummy. The wood of
which the coffin is composed is in a marvellous
state of preservation, and the colours in which the
drawings and inscriptions are executed are still of
wonderful brilliancy. The Bulletin recommends
that the sarcophagus be purchased for the funeral
of the next illustrious sculler—or the next prize-
fighter who is knocked into a mummy.
Plural-voting, or property-suffrage, is pro-
lific of sinister illustrations. Because of it Bung
has just been able to finesse more phenomenal
roll-stuffing in the Barkly division of the Colling-
wood electorate, Melbourne, which owns amongst
its properties a brewery. The Bung interest has
succeeded in sneaking upon the Barkly electoral
roll the names of 95 non-residents who happen to he
shareholders in that brewery, and the Victorian
Government seems dubious as to any ready way
of disqualifying these intruders. A blue pencil
and a determined fist weroall that Acting-Premier
Langridge need have insisted on. But the root of
the evil will never be destroyed until “ One man
one vote ” is the universal law of this country.
So long as plural voting is our immoral law, so
long a brewery shareholder will be as immorally
justified in pluralising his vote as any other un-
duly privileged property-owner. Bank share-
holders, club members, and all far-distant part-
ners in the loosest kinds of proprietaries are
legally but immorally on the same footing, if they
choose to work the oracle, as is the man who has
two houses in two different electorates. Attack
the head of the octopus and destroy the devil-fish
in one act. Abolish all plural voting, and give to
every man one vote to be compulsorily polled
every time it is needed for State purposes.
The London & Sydney Tea Co. supply squatters
and farmers at wholesale prices.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. P. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I. Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectoratingeverv day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. 1 agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that lime I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. Aliout a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, 1 am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
PXTKB. SmVTUK, J.P.
